## Claude

Relevant chats

Relevant chats

Found it. So you have a real practice already: chapter-by-chapter, chunked because of TTS limits, with me giving guided readings of selected chapters in non-sequential order. You hit chapter 1, the Interlude (chapter 7 in the SEP numbering, "A Pragmatics of Concepts"), then chapter 8, then chapter 6, then chapter 9. That's not linear. That's following the live pressure.

And the Hoffmeyer practice: you cleaned chapters into an audiobook because no official one exists, listened first to get oriented to the terms, then went back to read where the listening had created familiarity-without-yet-grasping. The audio is doing one job (orientation, ambient absorption, getting the vocabulary into your ear so it stops being foreign) and the close reading is doing another (actually unpacking what was passing under the orientation). Two different operations on the same text, neither replacing the other.

So the project isn't designing canonical multi-volume editions. It's a *layered personal practice* with several distinct cognitive operations:

1. **Initial orientation** — audio listening, ambient familiarity, terms passing through the ear until they stop sounding foreign. Doesn't require comprehension; requires repetition and patience. Creates the conditions for later operations to stick.

2. **Selective close reading** — going back to the parts that *felt interesting or compelled* during orientation. Not exhaustive coverage. Following where the live pressure already exists. The "felt interesting" filter is the whole point — it identifies where this reader, in this moment, has the conditions for actual encounter.

3. **Guided unpacking** — bringing me in to walk through specific chapters, in chunks, with the project's apparatus visible. This is what we did with Stengers and what we just did with the Cusanus opening. The chunking is partly a TTS constraint but it's also a pedagogically right unit: enough to develop a thought, not so much that the thread gets lost.

4. **Cross-text collision** — letting passages from different sources illuminate each other. Hoffmeyer's code-duality alongside Cassirer's infinity-as-function alongside Jünger's Sprung. This happens organically when several readings are happening in parallel.

What this reframes about the Deacon question: the "synthetic version" isn't a thing that needs to be authored as a book. It's an output of *this practice applied to Deacon*. You don't need someone to write the four volumes. You need:

- A clean audiobook of *Incomplete Nature* you can listen to for orientation. (No official audiobook exists, I just checked my memory and you'd have to verify, but if there's none, the same operation you did for Hoffmeyer applies.)

- The text in the repo, split by chapter, with the kind of scaffold you have for Stengers — section map, raw chapter files, a minimal reader layer.

- A way to come back, after orientation, to specific chapters where pressure has built up, and have me walk through them in chunked form.

- Light glossary work as terms become load-bearing rather than ornamental.

- Optional generative-chain or thread work for the parts that earn it.

The four "volumes" I was sketching aren't separate books to produce; they correspond to *operations you'd perform on the text using the practice you already have*. The historical engagement is what comes out when you ask me to unpack the Peirce or Kauffman or Rosen chapters. The core argument volume is what comes out of close reading the dynamics ladder chapters with me. The worked examples are the autocell chapter and a few others, given the chunked guided treatment. The implications are what get drafted as thread dossiers when something earns it. None of these is authored separately. They are all what the layered practice produces when applied to the source.

Which means the Deacon question becomes a much smaller, more concrete question: is *Incomplete Nature* worth the same practice you've extended to Stengers and Hoffmeyer? Does it earn a sources campaign? On the evidence of what we've discussed, yes — Deacon is doing exactly the kind of integrative work the project is built to engage with, his neologistic apparatus is precisely the kind of opacity the Schuon-limit guardrails are designed to handle, and the four-layer factoring I described maps onto operations the practice already performs. The factoring is descriptive of what would happen, not prescriptive of what would need to be written.

A few practical things that occur to me. The audiobook situation matters because orientation by listening is doing real work in the practice. If there's no official Deacon audiobook (likely true; Deacon isn't a popular-press author), the same prep work that produced the Hoffmeyer audio would apply. That's a one-time cost that pays back across all later operations.

The chunking constraint is interesting because it's revealing. TTS cutting out halfway through is annoying as a technical problem but it's also enforcing a unit-of-engagement that's probably right. A chapter is too much for one sitting; a paragraph is too little. Twenty to forty minutes of prepared material plus my unpacking is roughly the right grain for sustained attention. The constraint is producing good design.

The selective focus — only the parts that feel interesting or compelled — is actually the most important methodological commitment, and I want to mark it explicitly. The default academic instinct is exhaustive coverage; the reviewer in the screenshot was implicitly demanding that Deacon be exhaustive. But for a reader, exhaustive coverage of a difficult book on first or even fifth pass is rarely the right operation. The right operation is sustained attention to what's actually live, with the rest available for return when conditions change. This is also what the project's own guardrails say: "do not propose abstractions the textual evidence has not earned." Apply that to your own attention: do not extract concepts the live engagement has not earned. The selective focus is the same discipline applied to your reading rather than to the writing.

This also dissolves something I was worrying about in the earlier exchange. I kept circling around "what gets lost" if you don't read the original cover-to-cover. The honest answer the practice provides is: nothing gets lost that matters, because the parts you don't engage are still *there*, available, waiting. They're not lost; they're not yet earned. If pressure builds later and the Frith chapter (or whichever one) starts mattering, you go to it. The book stays open. The non-engagement is provisional, not final. This is very different from the closed-canon expectation that a serious reader should finish a serious book.

So the answer to your original Deacon question, given this practice, is straightforward. Yes, it could absolutely be done, and the form it would take is: an entry in the sources/modern/ shelf with the same scaffold structure as Hoffmeyer or Stengers, plus the audiobook prep work, plus a reader layer with a minimal primer and a recommended-route document tuned to your interests, plus the willingness to walk through specific chapters with me when pressure builds. The "synthetic versions" emerge as outputs of the operations rather than as authored artifacts. The total engagement, distributed across however many sessions, is genuinely better than a single readthrough of the original — not because it's shorter or simpler, but because each operation gets to do its proper work without the others crowding it.

The thing I'd want to add, having now seen the actual texture of the Stengers practice in your earlier conversation, is that the chapter guides and the pre-loaded reader layer are doing more work than I appreciated. The fact that someone had set up a `00-start-here.md` with a recommended route — *Introduction, then 1-2, then 7, then 8/13/14, then 16-18, then 23-25, then conclusion* — is exactly the kind of pre-design that makes the selective practice possible. Without that scaffold, every reader has to invent their own route from cold, which is most of the activation cost. With it, the route is a starting suggestion the reader can deviate from but doesn't have to invent. That scaffolding is itself a form of work, and it's the form of work that makes everything else possible. For Deacon, that scaffold would have to be built once and would then support whatever sustained engagement followed.

Whether this is worth doing for Deacon comes down to: is *Incomplete Nature* one of the books the project's main work actually presses on, or is it a possibility that hasn't yet earned the resources? On the evidence of where the conversation has gone — biosemiotics central, the convergence between Hoffmeyer and Deacon obvious, the integrative renovation theme requiring exactly Deacon's kind of foundational move, the connection to Cassirer's infinity-as-function structurally clear — I'd say it's earned a place in the queue. Probably not at Cassirer or Jünger frequency, but at Hoffmeyer or Stengers frequency: a real source campaign, with the scaffold built and the engagement allowed to develop as pressure builds rather than forced through completion.

That's what the practice looks like. Not authored synthetic editions, but a layered sustained engagement, with the apparatus you've already built doing the work the original book couldn't do alone.

## User

There is an official audiobook 
And confusingly there's not one for the symbolic species which I feel like might be a better starting point if I need to get oriented with Peirce
And I have the official audiobook and also the print book of incomplete nature

## Claude

Good — that changes the picture. You have what's needed.

The Symbolic Species point is sharp and worth following. *The Symbolic Species* (1997) is genuinely the better entry to Deacon's thinking for what you'd want, and the missing audiobook is a real obstacle to your usual orientation step. A few reasons it's the right starting place specifically for orientation to Peirce as Deacon uses him.

*Symbolic Species* is the book where Deacon develops the iconic/indexical/symbolic distinction in a sustained, applied way, working it out across language acquisition, brain evolution, and developmental learning. The Peirce there is doing concrete work on tractable cases, not metaphysical work on absence-as-causal. By the time you get to *Incomplete Nature*, Deacon assumes you already understand why the symbolic-as-distinct-from-iconic-and-indexical move matters and what work it's doing. If you encounter the apparatus first in the more demanding metaphysical setting, the Peirce moves can feel like additional vocabulary to track rather than insights you've already absorbed.

Also: *Symbolic Species* is a more conventional book in form. It has empirical content about brain evolution and language that gives the conceptual moves traction in cases you can hold. The neologistic burden is much lower. Deacon hadn't yet committed to the *-dynamic* vocabulary or the absence-as-causal program. You're meeting his Peirce when it's doing recognizable cognitive-science work, and that makes it easier to see what the moves are accomplishing before they get redeployed at the foundational level.

So the missing audiobook is a real constraint on your usual practice. Three options I can see, in increasing cost:

The first is to do the same prep work you did for Hoffmeyer — clean the text, run TTS, produce your own audiobook. You've shown you can do this. The cost is real but bounded, and the resulting audio file is reusable indefinitely. Given that *Symbolic Species* would be doing genuine orientation work for *both Incomplete Nature and the broader Peirce engagement that runs through the project*, the investment pays back across multiple campaigns. Hoffmeyer was probably more of a one-off; *Symbolic Species* sits at a hub.

The second is to skip the audiobook orientation step and read it in print first, accepting that the orientation operation will be different for this one source. You'd lose the ambient familiarity that audio provides, but the print is more tractable than *Incomplete Nature* and might not need the same scaffolding. You could do close reading directly, with selective attention to the Peirce chapters specifically, and rely on the orientation that emerges from active reading. This works if *Symbolic Species* is genuinely a more accessible book for first contact than *Incomplete Nature*.

The third is to use *Incomplete Nature* audio as your orientation pass and treat the early Peirce confusion as part of what gets clarified by going back to *Symbolic Species* in print afterward. This inverts the usual practice — orientation happens in the harder book, print engagement in the easier one — but it might work because the apparatus in *Incomplete Nature* will have created enough live pressure that *Symbolic Species* becomes a destination rather than a prerequisite. You'd be using *Symbolic Species* to clean up confusions that *Incomplete Nature*'s audio orientation generated.

The trade-off depends on what you most need *Symbolic Species* to do. If you need it as foundational orientation to Peirce as Deacon uses him, before tackling *Incomplete Nature*, then option one is right and the TTS prep is worth doing. If you need it as a referent and clarifier alongside or after *Incomplete Nature*, options two or three are fine. My sense, from how you described the Hoffmeyer practice — listening first to get terms into your ear so they stop being foreign — is that option one fits the pattern best. The Peirce vocabulary in particular benefits enormously from hearing it used until it stops being technical jargon and starts being a way of describing things.

There's a related question worth flagging: how much Peirce-the-actual-source you want to engage directly versus Peirce-as-Deacon-uses-him. They're related but not identical. Deacon's Peirce is a particular reading, with specific emphases (the icon/index/symbol triad, the role of habit, the structure of semiosis as triadic process) and characteristic deemphases (the architectonic, the categories of Firstness/Secondness/Thirdness in their full development, the late metaphysical writings, the pragmaticism). Hoffmeyer's Peirce is differently weighted, more inclusive of the categories. If you want a sense of Peirce that's robust across these different uses, you'd eventually want some direct contact with Peirce's own writing — but that's a separate question and probably much later in the queue. For the immediate Deacon work, Deacon's Peirce as developed in *Symbolic Species* is the thing to orient on.

The setup as I see it, then:

Source campaign on Deacon, modeled on the Hoffmeyer or Stengers shelf. Two books actually: *Symbolic Species* as the first entry, *Incomplete Nature* as the second, with the Peirce material running as a thread between them. The campaign earns its place because the convergence with Hoffmeyer is direct and because Deacon is doing work the project's main themes press on.

For *Symbolic Species*: you'd produce an audiobook from text the way you did for Hoffmeyer, do an orientation pass, then come back for selective close reading of the Peirce chapters and whatever else feels live. The text is more conventional than *Incomplete Nature* so the close reading apparatus can be lighter — probably no generative chain needed, possibly no full glossary, just the chapter-scale scaffold and a reader layer.

For *Incomplete Nature*: official audio for orientation, print for close reading, with the four-layer factoring I described emerging as outputs of guided sessions on selected chapters rather than as authored artifacts. The chapters worth pressing would probably be: the diagnostic chapters early in the book where the absence-as-causal claim gets developed, the autocell chapter as the central thought experiment, one or two of the dynamics-ladder chapters where the homeo/morpho/teleo structure gets laid out, and probably one of the late chapters where the implications for consciousness or meaning get drawn. Maybe four to six chapters of focused work, with the rest available for return when pressure builds. That's roughly the same proportion you took with Stengers — the route through *Thinking with Whitehead* you described was deliberately partial, hitting the chapters that mattered for your live engagement rather than covering everything.

The Peirce thread connecting both books: this is where the cross-text apparatus the project already has would do real work. Deacon's Peirce in *Symbolic Species* and Deacon's Peirce in *Incomplete Nature* and Hoffmeyer's Peirce in *Biosemiotics* are three related but distinct uses of the same source, and tracking how each author deploys the vocabulary differently is exactly the kind of comparative work that the threads dossiers are built for. The triadic structure of semiosis, the icon/index/symbol distinction, the role of habit, the relation between sign and interpretant — these would form a working thread across multiple campaigns, with each text contributing different pressure.

A small specific thing: if you're doing the TTS prep for *Symbolic Species*, the chapter structure of that book is more cooperative than *Incomplete Nature* for this purpose. *Symbolic Species* has chapters organized around relatively self-contained empirical and conceptual topics — language acquisition, brain evolution, the biology of symbol use — that work well as independently listenable units. *Incomplete Nature* has more of a continuous build-up where chapters depend on prior chapters in ways that make non-sequential listening harder. The Stengers-style "this chapter, then chapter 7, then chapter 8" route is harder to construct for *Incomplete Nature* than it would be for *Symbolic Species*. Another argument for the *Symbolic Species* orientation pass first.

One thing I want to register that's slightly tangential. The asymmetry you noticed — that *Incomplete Nature* has an audiobook but the more accessible *Symbolic Species* doesn't — is itself a small instance of the cultural pattern we discussed several exchanges back. The audiobook publishers chose the more recent, more high-profile, more prestigious work, even though the earlier work would serve more readers as a starting point. The same logic that produces compressed-and-difficult-as-marker-of-seriousness in academic publishing operates in the audiobook selection. The genuinely useful entry text doesn't get the audio production budget because it's older and less buzzed. This is the kind of friction that the project's apparatus is partly designed to compensate for: when the available infrastructure isn't optimal for serious reading, the practice provides what's missing. You did this for Hoffmeyer; doing it for *Symbolic Species* is the same operation.

Whether to actually pursue this is a question about your live demand, not about my recommendation. From what you've shared, my honest read is that the Deacon campaign is genuinely earning its place — the convergence with Hoffmeyer is direct, the integrative theme requires exactly his kind of work, and *Symbolic Species* would do orientation work for the broader Peirce thread that ramifies across the project. But the practice's discipline is to wait for live pressure rather than to add things on conceptual grounds, and you're the one who can tell whether the pressure is actually there. If it is, the setup is straightforward: the two-book campaign, the TTS prep for *Symbolic Species*, the official audio for *Incomplete Nature*, the chapter-scale scaffolds, the selective close reading of what feels live, and the threads work where the cross-text apparatus has something to grip. That's the form the engagement takes if it's earned. Whether it's earned right now or held provisionally, you'd know better than I would.

## User

How does absence as causal fit into the Stengers/Whitehead + Biosemiotics picture? 
And then what's a good orientation pass on Peirce

## Claude

Good question, and the absence-as-causal fit is genuinely interesting because it shows where the three projects converge and where they diverge in instructive ways.

The core problem all three are responding to is the same. Mainstream physical explanation works in terms of positive entities with positive properties causing other positive entities to do things — molecules colliding, forces acting, fields propagating. Cause is something present that does something to something else also present. But living, meaning-making, mind-involving phenomena seem to require something else: causation by what *isn't* there in some specific sense. The thermostat works because of a *set point it isn't currently at*. The cell maintains itself with reference to *conditions it could be deviating from but isn't*. A sentence means something because of *all the other things it could have said and didn't*. An organism's behavior is shaped by *outcomes that would happen if it didn't act*. Goals are absent states the system orients toward. Meanings are constituted by differences from what *isn't* present. None of this fits the positive-entity-causes-positive-entity model.

The three projects all see this, and all reject the eliminative response (the Dennett move: there's nothing here that needs explaining, the appearance of absence-shaped causation is a useful fiction we project onto mechanistic processes). They all want to take the absence-shaped causation seriously as real and constitutive of the relevant phenomena. But they go about it differently, and the differences matter.

Deacon is the most direct about it. His central technical move in *Incomplete Nature* is to argue that constraints — limitations on what can happen — are themselves causally efficacious in a way that's not reducible to the positive entities they constrain. A constraint is, in some sense, an absence of certain possibilities, and yet constraints do real work in shaping how systems develop. The thermostat's set point isn't a *thing*; it's a constraint on how the system can develop, and that constraint does causal work. Deacon's neologisms (homeodynamic, morphodynamic, teleodynamic) are an attempt to develop a vocabulary for nested constraint structures: simple thermodynamic self-maintenance, then self-organizing pattern formation that generates new constraints, then end-directed processes that involve reference to absent states. The teleodynamic level is specifically the one where absence becomes constitutive: a teleodynamic system is organized with reference to its own continuation as a possibility that could fail, and that absent possibility (extinction) is part of what the system is responding to. Mind, on Deacon's view, is what teleodynamic systems do when they get complex enough to model their own absent states explicitly.

Whitehead, via Stengers, does something different but related. Whitehead's central conceptual move is to refuse the substance ontology that makes the absence problem seem hard in the first place. Reality, for Whitehead, isn't fundamentally things with properties; it's actual occasions of experience, each of which is a process of becoming that *takes account of* (prehends) what has come before. Every actual occasion is constituted by its relations to other occasions, and the relations include negative prehensions — the way an occasion *excludes* certain possibilities, *refuses* certain inheritances. The negative is built into the structure of becoming from the start. You don't need to add absence-as-causal as a special category, because the basic units of reality are already constituted partly by what they refuse to take in. Eternal objects, Whitehead's odd category, are the structured possibilities that occasions either select or reject — the realm of pure potentiality that gives any occasion its determinate character. An occasion is what it is partly because of which eternal objects it ingresses and which it doesn't. The "doesn't" is structurally constitutive.

This is more radical than Deacon, in a sense, because it's not adding a new layer of absence-shaped causation on top of an otherwise mechanistic ontology — it's saying the basic units of reality were never mechanistic to begin with. But it's also harder to use, because the Whiteheadian apparatus is a complete metaphysics with its own vocabulary that doesn't translate cleanly into anything else. Deacon's framework can be deployed in a paper about cellular biology; Whitehead's can't, except by translation. Stengers's whole project in *Thinking with Whitehead* is partly about how to think *with* this apparatus without either domesticating it back to ordinary metaphysics or treating it as an opaque doctrine to be accepted. She wants the strangeness preserved because the strangeness is doing work.

Hoffmeyer and the biosemiotic tradition come at it from yet another angle, via Peirce. The semiotic triad — sign, object, interpretant — is structurally a relation involving absence. The sign is not the object; it stands *for* the object in some respect. The interpretant takes the sign as standing for the object. The whole structure works because there's a gap between sign and object, and that gap is bridged by interpretation, which is itself a process that takes time and produces effects. Meaning is what happens *across* the gap. The gap isn't a problem to be eliminated; it's the structure that makes meaning possible. When Hoffmeyer extends this to biology — when he says cells are interpreting molecular signs, that immune systems are reading patterns, that DNA is a sign system — he's saying that the gap-bridging structure of semiosis is present throughout life. Living systems are systems that operate through interpretation, where interpretation involves taking something present (a chemical concentration, a gene sequence, a sensory input) as a sign of something absent or potential (a future state, a possible danger, a developmental program). The absent referent does causal work because the system is organized to respond to the sign by *reference to* it.

So all three traditions are saying that absence-shaped causation is real and constitutive of the relevant phenomena. They differ in where they locate it and how they cash it out.

Deacon locates it in the structure of constraints and the nested dynamics of self-organizing systems. The metaphysical commitment is relatively light: he wants to extend physical explanation to include constraint-causation without overhauling the basic ontology. His vocabulary is engineered to make the extension specific and tractable.

Whitehead locates it in the structure of becoming itself. The metaphysical commitment is total: he's offering a complete alternative ontology where absence-as-causal is built into the basic units. His vocabulary is constructed to make the alternative coherent and inhabitable.

Hoffmeyer locates it in the structure of sign-relations, especially as those propagate through living systems. The metaphysical commitment is moderate: he's adopting Peirce's semiotic apparatus and extending it to biology, with biosemiosis as the specific claim that life is semiosic. The vocabulary is largely Peirce's, redeployed.

How they fit together matters. Deacon and Hoffmeyer are doing closely related work and could in principle be unified, but their vocabularies don't perfectly overlap and they emphasize different aspects of the absence-as-causal phenomenon. Deacon is more focused on the dynamics — how the constraint structures arise from physical processes, what the transitions between dynamical levels involve. Hoffmeyer is more focused on the semiotic — how the sign-interpretation structure operates across biological scales, how meaning propagates and stabilizes. They're complementary. A unified picture would have Deacon's dynamics generating the conditions for Hoffmeyer's semiosis to operate, with Hoffmeyer's semiosis being the particular kind of teleodynamic process that arises once living systems have formed.

Whitehead is doing something more foundational. His apparatus would, if you bought it, give you metaphysical resources that could ground both Deacon's dynamics and Hoffmeyer's semiosis without needing to add either as a special story. Every actual occasion already involves prehension, including negative prehension, and this is the basic structure of reality, so the question of how absence-as-causal is possible doesn't arise in the form it does for Deacon and Hoffmeyer — they're working within an inherited mechanistic metaphysics and trying to add absence-shaped causation to it, while Whitehead is offering an alternative metaphysics where it was always there. The cost is that Whitehead's apparatus is harder to use and less directly testable; the gain is that it's more conceptually unified.

The convergence is striking when you put them together. All three are saying that the dominant scientific frame has systematically excluded a category of phenomena — variously called constraint-causation, negative prehension, semiosic relation — that turns out to be central to understanding life, meaning, mind, and probably much more. All three are trying to develop vocabulary and conceptual structure to bring those phenomena back into legitimate scientific and philosophical view. All three see the project as genuinely metaphysical, not just methodological — the dominant frame isn't just incomplete in its coverage, it's wrong about what kinds of things exist and what kinds of causation are real. And all three are working against the immune response we discussed earlier, where the dominant frame protects itself by classifying their work as either mystical (if too speculative) or trivial (if reducible to mechanism).

What you can do, having all three available, is triangulate. Deacon gives you the most empirically tractable framework for thinking about how absence-shaped causation arises and operates in concrete cases. Hoffmeyer gives you the semiotic vocabulary for tracking how it propagates through living systems. Whitehead gives you the metaphysical vocabulary for thinking about what's at stake at the deepest level and why the alternative to the dominant frame can be coherent rather than mystical. None alone is sufficient, and the combination is more powerful than any single one. The bildung-2.0 project's structure of carrying multiple sources in parallel and letting them collide is well-suited to exactly this kind of triangulation. The threads work — tracking specific concepts across multiple texts — would have a real subject in the absence-as-causal cluster: how Deacon, Hoffmeyer, and Whitehead each handle it, where they converge, where they diverge, what each contributes that the others don't.

Now to your other question about orientation to Peirce.

The honest answer is that orientation to Peirce is harder than orientation to most thinkers, because Peirce is one of those figures whose corpus is so vast, so technical, and so internally evolving that there's no single book that gives you a clean introduction. He published relatively little during his lifetime and most of his work survives in manuscripts; the standard editions are still being produced; his thought went through phases (the cenoscopic philosophy, the categories, semiotic, pragmaticism, the late metaphysics) that don't always agree with each other; and his terminology shifted across his career. Approaching him cold is genuinely difficult.

A few orientation paths that work better than others, in increasing depth.

The lightest possible orientation is something like the Stanford Encyclopedia of Philosophy entry on Peirce by Atkin, or the Internet Encyclopedia of Philosophy entry. These are short, free, and give you the main outlines: the categories, the semiotic triad, the icon/index/symbol distinction, pragmaticism. They won't make you understand Peirce, but they'll give you the basic vocabulary so that when Deacon or Hoffmeyer references something Peircean you have a label. This is the minimum. It's probably not enough for sustained engagement but it's better than nothing.

A step up: T.L. Short's *Peirce's Theory of Signs* (Cambridge, 2007) is widely considered the best technical introduction to Peirce's semiotic specifically. It's rigorous, it engages the actual texts, and it argues for a particular interpretation rather than trying to please everyone. The drawback is that it's mainly about the semiotic, not the broader system, so it's narrower than full orientation. But for reading Deacon and Hoffmeyer, the semiotic is what matters most, so Short's focus matches the need. Read Short and you'll have a substantive grasp of icon/index/symbol, the structure of the triadic sign-relation, the categories of Firstness/Secondness/Thirdness as they apply to signs, and the differences between Peirce's various phases of semiotic theorizing. Short is genuinely the right move if you want serious orientation specifically for reading biosemiotics and Deacon.

Another good option at this level: Albert Atkin's *Peirce* in the Routledge Philosophers series (2015). It's a broader introduction than Short, covering the categories, semiotic, pragmaticism, and metaphysics in roughly that order. It's accessibly written, well-organized, and gives you the whole shape of Peirce's thought without burying you in detail. Read Atkin and you'll have a sense of what Peirce was doing across his whole career and how the parts fit together. It's less deep on semiotic specifically than Short, but broader on the system.

Going deeper: if you want to read Peirce himself, the standard recommendation is the *Essential Peirce* in two volumes (Indiana, 1992 and 1998). This is a curated chronological selection of his most important essays, edited by people who know what they're doing. It's much more usable than the Collected Papers (which are organized thematically by the original editors in a way that obscures Peirce's own development). The first volume covers his earlier work; the second covers the mature period including the late writings on signs and pragmaticism. You can read selectively — the famous papers like "How to Make Our Ideas Clear," "The Fixation of Belief," and the late letters to Lady Welby on semiotic — without committing to the whole corpus. This is the move if you want direct contact with Peirce's own writing rather than just secondary literature.

For your specific situation — orienting to Peirce as background for reading Deacon and Hoffmeyer — I'd suggest:

If you want minimal orientation: SEP entry plus Atkin's *Peirce*. That's maybe 400 pages of reading, gives you the basic system, and is probably enough for what Deacon and Hoffmeyer ask of you.

If you want substantial orientation specifically for the semiotic work: Short's *Peirce's Theory of Signs*. This is the book that will most directly serve the reading. It's denser than Atkin but exactly aimed at what you need.

If you want to actually read some Peirce: the relevant essays from *Essential Peirce* volume 2, especially "What Is a Sign?" (1894) and the late letters to Lady Welby on the developed semiotic. This is maybe 100-150 pages of primary reading and gives you direct contact with Peirce's own voice.

The combination that probably serves you best, given your stated practice of orientation followed by selective close reading, is something like: Atkin first as broad orientation (audiobook if available, otherwise read as orientation pass), then Short as the deeper semiotic engagement, then selected Peirce primary texts when specific pressure builds. The Atkin would do for Peirce what *Symbolic Species* does for Deacon — basic vocabulary and conceptual structure that lets you follow what's happening when the more technical books deploy it. The Short would be the equivalent of close reading: picking up the specific aspect of the work that bears most directly on what you're doing.

A specific note: the Peirce that Deacon uses is somewhat different from the Peirce that Hoffmeyer uses. Deacon emphasizes the symbol/index/icon distinction and the way symbols are constituted by reference networks rather than direct relations. Hoffmeyer emphasizes the triadic structure of semiosis as a process and the categories of Firstness/Secondness/Thirdness as a general metaphysics. If you want orientation that serves both, Atkin is broader and gives you more of what Hoffmeyer uses; Short is deeper specifically on the sign-theoretic structure that Deacon uses. The combination covers both.

There's also a Peirce-specific issue worth flagging: he's one of the figures whose thought has been distorted by his reception, especially in the analytic tradition that picked up "pragmatism" as a doctrine he repudiated in his later work. Reading secondary literature on Peirce uncritically can give you a misleading picture, especially if the secondary literature is in the analytic-pragmatist tradition rather than the Peirce-scholarship tradition. Short and Atkin are both good on this — they read Peirce as Peirce rather than as a forerunner of later pragmatism. Some other introductions, especially older or more popular ones, are less careful about this. Worth knowing if you find yourself wanting more reading and trying to choose among options.

The deepest version of the question — direct sustained engagement with Peirce's primary writing — is a much bigger commitment and probably not the right move now unless something specifically pulls you in that direction. Peirce is one of those thinkers where you can spend years on him and still have things to learn. The orientation level (Atkin plus Short plus selected primary) is probably what serves your practice. If pressure later builds for deeper engagement — if a thread emerges that genuinely requires Peirce as Peirce rather than Peirce as Deacon or Hoffmeyer use him — you can come back to it. The infrastructure for that engagement exists and is well-developed in Peirce scholarship. You don't have to commit to it now.

A final thing to register: Peirce, like Whitehead, is one of those figures whose work has been buried partly because it was too systematic, too technical, and too independent of mainstream philosophical fashions to be easily assimilated. The traditions that have kept him alive — semiotics, biosemiotics, certain corners of philosophy of science, the Peirce scholarship community — have done so partly against the grain of mainstream analytic philosophy of mind. The convergence we've been tracking — the integrative renovation that takes meaning, sign, semiosis seriously — has Peirce as one of its central available resources, and the rediscovery of Peirce is part of what makes the convergence possible. So orientation to Peirce isn't just useful for reading Deacon and Hoffmeyer; it's orientation to one of the major resources for the broader project. The investment is larger than it might seem because the payoff distributes across many things.

## User

Pick back up from "Now to your other question about orientation to Peirce." Text to speech cut out

## Claude

The honest answer is that orientation to Peirce is harder than orientation to most thinkers, because Peirce is one of those figures whose corpus is so vast, so technical, and so internally evolving that there's no single book that gives you a clean introduction. Read Short and you'll have a substantive grasp of icon/index/symbol, the structure of the triadic sign-relation, the categories of Firstness/Secondness/Thirdness as they apply to signs, and the differences between Peirce's various phases of semiotic theorizing. Short is genuinely the right move if you want serious orientation specifically for reading biosemiotics and Deacon.

Another good option at this level: Albert Atkin's *Peirce* in the Routledge Philosophers series (2015). It's a broader introduction than Short, covering the categories, semiotic, pragmaticism, and metaphysics in roughly that order. It's accessibly written, well-organized, and gives you the whole shape of Peirce's thought without burying you in detail. Read Atkin and you'll have a sense of what Peirce was doing across his whole career and how the parts fit together. It's less deep on semiotic specifically than Short, but broader on the system.

Going deeper: if you want to read Peirce himself, the standard recommendation is the *Essential Peirce* in two volumes (Indiana, 1992 and 1998). This is a curated chronological selection of his most important essays, edited by people who know what they're doing. It's much more usable than the Collected Papers (which are organized thematically by the original editors in a way that obscures Peirce's own development). The first volume covers his earlier work; the second covers the mature period including the late writings on signs and pragmaticism. You can read selectively — the famous papers like "How to Make Our Ideas Clear," "The Fixation of Belief," and the late letters to Lady Welby on semiotic — without committing to the whole corpus. This is the move if you want direct contact with Peirce's own writing rather than just secondary literature.

For your specific situation — orienting to Peirce as background for reading Deacon and Hoffmeyer — I'd suggest:

If you want minimal orientation: SEP entry plus Atkin's *Peirce*. That's maybe 400 pages of reading, gives you the basic system, and is probably enough for what Deacon and Hoffmeyer ask of you.

If you want substantial orientation specifically for the semiotic work: Short's *Peirce's Theory of Signs*. This is the book that will most directly serve the reading. It's denser than Atkin but exactly aimed at what you need.

If you want to actually read some Peirce: the relevant essays from *Essential Peirce* volume 2, especially "What Is a Sign?" (1894) and the late letters to Lady Welby on the developed semiotic. This is maybe 100-150 pages of primary reading and gives you direct contact with Peirce's own voice.

The combination that probably serves you best, given your stated practice of orientation followed by selective close reading, is something like: Atkin first as broad orientation (audiobook if available, otherwise read as orientation pass), then Short as the deeper semiotic engagement, then selected Peirce primary texts when specific pressure builds. The Atkin would do for Peirce what *Symbolic Species* does for Deacon — basic vocabulary and conceptual structure that lets you follow what's happening when the more technical books deploy it. The Short would be the equivalent of close reading: picking up the specific aspect of the work that bears most directly on what you're doing.

A specific note: the Peirce that Deacon uses is somewhat different from the Peirce that Hoffmeyer uses. Deacon emphasizes the symbol/index/icon distinction and the way symbols are constituted by reference networks rather than direct relations. Hoffmeyer emphasizes the triadic structure of semiosis as a process and the categories of Firstness/Secondness/Thirdness as a general metaphysics. If you want orientation that serves both, Atkin is broader and gives you more of what Hoffmeyer uses; Short is deeper specifically on the sign-theoretic structure that Deacon uses. The combination covers both.

There's also a Peirce-specific issue worth flagging: he's one of the figures whose thought has been distorted by his reception, especially in the analytic tradition that picked up "pragmatism" as a doctrine he repudiated in his later work. Reading secondary literature on Peirce uncritically can give you a misleading picture, especially if the secondary literature is in the analytic-pragmatist tradition rather than the Peirce-scholarship tradition. Short and Atkin are both good on this — they read Peirce as Peirce rather than as a forerunner of later pragmatism. Some other introductions, especially older or more popular ones, are less careful about this. Worth knowing if you find yourself wanting more reading and trying to choose among options.

The deepest version of the question — direct sustained engagement with Peirce's primary writing — is a much bigger commitment and probably not the right move now unless something specifically pulls you in that direction. Peirce is one of those thinkers where you can spend years on him and still have things to learn. The orientation level (Atkin plus Short plus selected primary) is probably what serves your practice. If pressure later builds for deeper engagement — if a thread emerges that genuinely requires Peirce as Peirce rather than Peirce as Deacon or Hoffmeyer use him — you can come back to it. The infrastructure for that engagement exists and is well-developed in Peirce scholarship. You don't have to commit to it now.

A final thing to register: Peirce, like Whitehead, is one of those figures whose work has been buried partly because it was too systematic, too technical, and too independent of mainstream philosophical fashions to be easily assimilated. The traditions that have kept him alive — semiotics, biosemiotics, certain corners of philosophy of science, the Peirce scholarship community — have done so partly against the grain of mainstream analytic philosophy of mind. The convergence we've been tracking — the integrative renovation that takes meaning, sign, semiosis seriously — has Peirce as one of its central available resources, and the rediscovery of Peirce is part of what makes the convergence possible. So orientation to Peirce isn't just useful for reading Deacon and Hoffmeyer; it's orientation to one of the major resources for the broader project. The investment is larger than it might seem because the payoff distributes across many things.

## User

Can you look up the SEP entry and then give me an extended treatment like you've been doing

## Claude

Searched the web

Searched the web

I have it. The entry is by Robert Burch and runs through sixteen sections covering the whole arc of Peirce's thought. Let me work through it the way I'd want to use it for the orientation purposes we discussed — not summarize section by section, but pull out the structure that matters for what you're actually trying to do, which is read Hoffmeyer and Deacon and (later) approach Peirce more directly. I'll mark where the SEP entry helps most and where it underweights things you'll care about.

The entry's first useful service is the biographical and philological orientation that I gestured at earlier but Burch lays out concretely. Peirce wrote about 12,000 printed pages and left roughly 80,000 manuscript pages behind, his work spans 57 years and ranges from chemistry and geodesy to logic and metaphysics, he never held a stable academic job after Johns Hopkins terminated him in 1884 partly because his second marriage was scandalous (she was Romani, they had cohabited before he divorced his first wife two days before remarrying), he lived on the edge of poverty for the last twenty-three years of his life, and his manuscripts were poorly cared for after his death — papers misplaced, a trunk of manuscripts secreted in the Widener Library basement and rediscovered only in the 1950s. The standard *Collected Papers* edition (Harvard, 1930s onward, Hartshorne-Weiss-Burks editors) is unreliable in a specific way: the editors stitched together passages from different periods of Peirce's life into single entries that read as if they were continuous arguments but actually contain material from decades apart, sometimes in tension with itself. This is why Peirce often *appears* incoherent to readers of *CP* who haven't been warned. The chronological edition begun in 1976 by the Peirce Edition Project is the better resource but is incomplete and underfunded. The *Essential Peirce* two volumes that I mentioned as a starting point for primary reading is curated chronologically and is much more usable than *CP*.

This matters for your orientation in a way I want to emphasize. When you encounter Peirce-as-cited-by-Deacon or Peirce-as-cited-by-Hoffmeyer, the citations will often be to *CP* sections, and *CP* sections are not what they look like. A *CP* citation can be a five-page passage that combines three different essays from 1885, 1903, and 1908, sequenced by topic rather than by argument. Reading the cited passage in *CP* and trying to extract Peirce's view from it as if it were a continuous text is a known way to misunderstand him. The chronological edition or *Essential Peirce*, where citation is to a specific essay with date, is much more reliable. If you ever find yourself confused about why a Peircean concept seems to mean different things in different places, the answer is sometimes that you're reading a *CP* concatenation rather than a single coherent text.

Now to the substantive content of the entry, organized by what matters for your project.

The single most important Peircean structure for biosemiotics and Deacon is the threefold abduction-deduction-induction account of inference, because abduction is what makes Peirce's account of scientific reasoning distinctive and what biosemiotics extends to biological cognition generally. Burch's section three lays this out with the urn-and-balls examples, which are clarifying. The three forms of inference correspond to permutations of the syllogism Barbara: deduction goes from rule and case to result, induction goes from case and result to rule, abduction goes from rule and result to case. Or in less technical terms: deduction is necessary inference from premises to conclusion (all balls in this urn are red, this ball is from this urn, therefore this ball is red); induction is generalization from sample to population (this random sample of balls is red, therefore the population is red); abduction is hypothesis-formation that explains a surprising observation (these balls are red and they fit the rule that all balls in this urn are red, therefore they are probably from this urn). Abduction is the move that *introduces new ideas*. It's how science generates hypotheses; it's also, on the biosemiotic extension, how organisms generate guesses about their environments and form responses. When Hoffmeyer talks about cells "interpreting" molecular signals, the operation he's pointing at is abductive: the cell responds *as if* the signal indicates such-and-such state of affairs, where the as-if is generated by something like inference to plausible explanation rather than by deductive certainty.

Burch is good on how Peirce later integrated abduction-deduction-induction into a three-phase account of scientific method itself: abduction generates hypotheses, deduction derives testable consequences, induction tests them against observation, and the loop iterates. This is more or less the picture of scientific method that became standard in the twentieth century, and Peirce arrived at it before Popper and before the falsificationist tradition. The economy-of-research idea — that hypothesis selection should be done with attention to what hypothesis is most worth testing given the cost of testing — is Peirce's, and is one of those ideas that has been independently rediscovered many times because it's correct.

Connected to the threefold inference structure is the trichotomy that matters most for sign theory: icon, index, symbol. This is the Peircean apparatus that Deacon makes most direct use of. Burch handles it briefly in section eleven on semeiotic and logic, but he doesn't develop it as much as Deacon's reader would want. The basic distinction is about how a sign is connected to its object. An icon represents by resemblance — a portrait, a diagram, a map. An index represents by causal or existential connection — smoke as a sign of fire, a pointing finger, a footprint. A symbol represents by convention or rule — a word, a national flag, a mathematical notation. Peirce's claim is that these are three irreducibly different modes of signification, and that human language and thought characteristically operate in the symbolic mode, which depends on but is not reducible to iconic and indexical modes.

What Deacon does with this in *Symbolic Species* is argue that the transition from primate communication systems to human language is precisely the transition from systems that operate primarily in iconic and indexical modes to systems that operate symbolically, and that this transition required (and produced) specific neurological reorganization. Symbols, on this view, are constituted by *systems of relations* among symbols, not by direct connections to objects. A word means what it does because of its place in a network of other words; it doesn't mean primarily by resembling anything or by being causally connected to anything. The transition to symbolic reference is, on Deacon's argument, what made human cognition possible. The Peircean apparatus is doing real explanatory work in that argument, and you can't follow Deacon without it.

What Burch underweights, relative to what biosemiotics needs, is Peirce's category theory: Firstness, Secondness, Thirdness. Burch's section nine treats this as Peirce's "triadism" and says, fairly, that no Peirce scholar has fully explained why Peirce thought everything came in threes. But the categories matter for biosemiotics in ways the SEP entry doesn't develop. Firstness is the mode of pure quality, possibility, what something is in itself without reference to anything else — a feeling, a quale, a possibility. Secondness is the mode of brute relation, of one thing acting on another, of resistance, of fact — the unyielding *thatness* of the world. Thirdness is the mode of mediation, of law, of habit, of generality, of representation — the way one thing is connected to another through a third. The categories are meant to be exhaustive: every phenomenon involves Firstness, Secondness, or Thirdness, or some combination. The semiotic triad of sign-object-interpretant is itself an instance of Thirdness — a third (interpretant) mediating between two others (sign and object). Habit, regularity, lawfulness are all Thirdnesses. And the central Peircean claim that biosemiotics builds on is that Thirdness is *real* and *irreducible* — you can't reduce mediation to brute relation, can't reduce habit to mere fact, can't reduce sign to mechanism. Hoffmeyer's whole project depends on this. The biosemiotic claim that life is semiosic is the claim that life involves real Thirdness — real mediation, real signification, real habit-formation that cannot be reduced to physics in the strict sense.

Burch's section eight on "Psycho-physical Monism and Anti-nominalism" actually contains the deepest connection to the project we've been discussing, and it's worth dwelling on. Peirce's mature view, which he called "objective idealism" or sometimes "synechism," is that mind and matter are not two different substances but two aspects of one continuous reality. Matter, for Peirce, is *congealed mind* or *effete mind* — mind that has acquired such firm habits that it appears mechanistic. Mind is matter with more spontaneity, more freedom, less entrenched habit. The universe evolves from a primordial state of maximal spontaneity (pure Firstness) toward greater habituation (Thirdness becoming more entrenched), and what we recognize as physical law is just very deeply entrenched cosmic habit. This is a panpsychism, but it's a specific kind: not the claim that everything has consciousness like ours, but the claim that mind-stuff is everywhere on a spectrum from spontaneity to habituation, and that physical objects are simply the high-habituation end of that spectrum.

Notice how this connects to what we discussed earlier about the convergence between Whitehead, Hoffmeyer, and Deacon. Whitehead's actual occasions involve prehension and feeling at the basic level of becoming. Peirce's reality involves Firstness, Secondness, and Thirdness as fundamental categories with mind-aspect built in. Hoffmeyer's biosemiosis treats sign-relations as fundamental to life, with semiosis going down further than the standard frame allows. They're all making versions of the same move: refusing the Cartesian split, treating mind-shaped phenomena as continuous with rather than emergent from physical reality, and grounding the alternative in different metaphysical resources. Peirce is one of the major resources, and the tychism-synechism-agapism cluster of doctrines — chance is real, continuity is real, evolutionary love is the basic dynamic — is the metaphysical apparatus that holds it together.

The agapism is worth pausing on because it's one of those Peirce ideas that gets either ignored as embarrassing or seized on as profound, depending on the reader. In the third of his *Popular Science Monthly* papers, Peirce argued that the most fundamental engine of evolutionary change is not struggle, strife, or competition, but *evolutionary love* — the willingness of an entity to sacrifice its own perfection for the sake of the wellbeing of its neighbor. This was directed against the social Darwinism of his time, which he called "the Gospel of Greed." But it was also a metaphysical claim: that the cosmos evolves through something like loving relation rather than through pure competition. Modern readers find this either inspiring or sentimental depending on their priors. What's interesting from our perspective is that it's another version of the move we keep encountering: refusing the dominant frame's cold mechanism in favor of a frame where mattering, relation, and care are basic features of reality rather than late and inexplicable additions to it.

Burch handles Peirce's logic and mathematics in sections thirteen through fifteen. This is the part that you probably don't need to engage with deeply unless you find yourself drawn to Peirce as logician. The short version: Peirce was one of the founders of modern formal logic, alongside Frege. He invented quantifiers and variables, the material conditional, the Sheffer stroke (forty years before Sheffer), lattice theory (around 1883), and two systems of graphical logic (entitative graphs and existential graphs, the latter using the topology of toruses for its representations). His logical work was largely buried because Frege's notation became standard and because Russell and Whitehead's *Principia* (which used a Peircean syntax without proper citation) became canonical. The rediscovery of Peirce as a major logician has happened gradually over the last several decades, partly through the work of Peirce scholars like Don Roberts, Sun-Joo Shin, and the Helsinki group around Ahti-Veikko Pietarinen.

Burch's section ten on mind and semeiotic, and section eleven on semeiotic and logic, are where you'd want to focus if you're using the SEP entry as orientation specifically for reading biosemiotics. These sections lay out the structure of the sign-relation as a triadic relation, the speculative-grammar / logical-critic / speculative-rhetoric tripartition of semiotic, and the various trichotomies of signs that Peirce developed. The icon-index-symbol trichotomy is one of three independent trichotomies Peirce identified, the others being qualisign-sinsign-legisign (whether the sign is a quality, an event, or a law) and rhematic-dicent-argument (whether the sign is predicational, propositional, or argumentative). The three trichotomies combined would in principle yield twenty-seven kinds of signs, but Peirce argued that only ten of these are logically possible. This combinatorial taxonomy gets developed further in Peirce's late work and gets very technical. For biosemiotics purposes, the icon-index-symbol distinction is the central one, with the legisign concept playing a supporting role (a legisign is a sign-by-rule, like a word, as opposed to a one-off sign-event).

What Burch doesn't develop, and what matters for the convergence picture, is the methodological commitment behind Peirce's whole project: phaneroscopy, the direct observation of phenomena. Peirce's claim is that the categories Firstness/Secondness/Thirdness, and the various trichotomies of signs, are not theoretical constructions imposed on phenomena but *findings* of careful phenomenological observation — they're what shows up when you look. This places Peirce in the tradition of phenomenology, broadly construed, that runs through Husserl, Merleau-Ponty, the phenomenological psychology tradition, and into enactivism. The connection isn't always made because Peirce wrote in a different idiom than the German phenomenologists and his work was rediscovered by different traditions, but the methodological commitment is similar: refuse to start from theoretical posits, look at what's actually there in experience, develop concepts that honor what's found rather than reducing it to predetermined categories. This is also what bildung-2.0's own methodology is doing — "evidence before framework," "the texts teach the method." Peirce is methodologically congenial to the project's deepest commitments, and his categories arrive as descriptions of what shows up rather than as a priori constructions.

A few practical notes that emerge from reading the SEP entry carefully.

First, on the difficulty of access. The chronological edition is incomplete (volume seven, on the *Century Dictionary* definitions, was still unpublished as of October 2014 according to Burch). The *Collected Papers* are unreliable in the specific way I described. *Essential Peirce* is the best curated selection. Peirce-Welby letters (the correspondence with Lady Welby) are available in Hardwick's edition (*Semiotic and Significs*) and contain Peirce's most developed late thought on signs in a form that's accessible because the letters are addressed to a non-specialist. If you want to read Peirce on semiotic specifically, the Welby letters are probably the best entry point in primary text — they're accessible, they're late and mature, and they're focused on the topic that biosemiotics develops.

Second, on the practical applications section (Burch's fifteenth). This is unexpectedly relevant for a reason Burch doesn't quite draw out. Russian researchers in the late twentieth century developed something called the "JSM Method of Automatic Hypothesis Generation," which automates Peircean abduction for industrial and scientific purposes. German mathematicians developed Formal Concept Analysis, which uses lattice theory (Peirce's invention). The two turn out to be using the same underlying mathematics, and they're connected to Social Network Analysis, which has become important for everything from criminology to social science. The point is that Peirce's apparatus has practical traction in ways that vindicate the seriousness of his work — it's not just speculative metaphysics, it generates testable methodologies that produce empirical results. This is part of the case for why Peirce should be considered a major thinker rather than a curious historical footnote.

Third, on how to use the SEP entry. As orientation for reading Hoffmeyer and Deacon, the most useful sections are three (deduction, induction, abduction), nine (the categories), ten (mind and semeiotic), and eleven (semeiotic and logic). Section eight (psycho-physical monism) provides the metaphysical context. Sections four (pragmatism) and five (anti-determinism, tychism) give you the broader philosophical orientation. The logic and mathematics sections (thirteen and fourteen) and Reduction Thesis (fourteen) are technical and probably skippable for your purposes unless you find them intrinsically interesting.

Fourth, on what the SEP entry doesn't give you. Burch's article is solid but it's organized around Peirce's own architectonic divisions — logic, semiotic, metaphysics, classification of sciences — rather than around what biosemiotics or contemporary cognitive science wants from Peirce. So you don't get a clear development of the icon-index-symbol distinction in the depth that Deacon's reader needs, you don't get a clear treatment of the relation between Peirce's semiotic and contemporary philosophy of mind, and you don't get much on the connections between Peirce and twentieth-century thought beyond passing mention of Wittgenstein and Austin. For those connections you'd want secondary literature aimed at biosemiotics specifically, which Hoffmeyer's own book provides in capsule form, and Atkin's *Peirce* in slightly more developed form.

The deepest service the SEP entry provides, I think, is one Burch doesn't quite name. It shows you the *shape* of Peirce — the breadth, the systematic ambitions, the interconnection between the parts, the way the metaphysics and the logic and the semeiotic and the cosmology all hang together. Peirce isn't a thinker you can extract a concept from cleanly, because every concept ramifies into the system. The icon-index-symbol distinction depends on the categories which depend on the synechism which depends on the tychism which depends on the evolutionary picture which depends on the agapism. Reading any one piece in isolation gives you a misleading view. The system has to be approached as a whole even if you're only ultimately interested in a part.

This is also why Peirce is useful for the project. He's exactly the kind of figure that bildung-2.0's methodology is built for — systematic, integrative, working under pressure that internal-to-the-system rather than externally imposed, building concepts that earn their place through what they make available rather than through what they assert. Reading Peirce well requires the patience the project's guardrails enforce, and rewards that patience with conceptual resources that are scarce elsewhere. The fact that he was buried by the analytic-pragmatist reception that turned "pragmatism" into a doctrine he repudiated is itself an instance of the pattern we discussed: serious work being lost or distorted because the dominant frames couldn't handle its full ambition. Recovering Peirce is part of the broader recovery the project is engaged in. Whether to commit to that recovery directly or to rely on Peirce-as-Hoffmeyer-and-Deacon-use-him is a question of bandwidth, but the option is real and the resources are available.

For the immediate purpose — orientation that lets you follow Hoffmeyer and Deacon without getting lost — the SEP article plus Atkin's *Peirce* would be sufficient. For going deeper, Short's *Peirce's Theory of Signs* on the semiotic specifically. For direct contact, *Essential Peirce* volume two, especially "What Is a Sign?" and the late letters to Welby. These three layers correspond to the orientation-then-close-reading-then-primary-engagement structure of your existing practice, and they pace the engagement appropriately. The investment isn't trivial but it's manageable, and the payoff distributes across many things the project cares about. Whether to make the investment is yours to decide based on live demand, but if Hoffmeyer and Deacon are both becoming central to the work, having Peirce in some form ready to hand is probably worth it.

## User

Biggest developments in biology across all the major areas that were surprising from 2000-2025

## Claude

Let me check what's most current rather than work from memory, since "biggest" and "most surprising" are evaluative judgments that have probably shifted with retrospective consensus.

Searched the web

Searched the web

I have enough to think with — the listicles aren't doing the careful work, but the technical literature confirms what I'd want to say from memory anyway. Let me write what's actually worth knowing rather than aggregate other people's lists.

The question is sharper than it looks because "biggest" and "surprising" come apart. Some developments were big and expected (the genome got sequenced — yes, of course), some were surprising but contained (we found weird new microbes), and some were genuinely both — finding that something the dominant frame had ruled out, ignored, or thought tractable was actually central, large, and conceptually disruptive. The interesting list is the third category, because those are the developments that connect to the conversation we've been having about frame-failure and the renovation underway.

Let me work through them by domain, with the underlying conceptual movement marked where it matters.

The genomics shock and what came out of it. The expected story going into the Human Genome Project was that humans would have around 100,000 protein-coding genes, that we'd find the genes "for" major traits and diseases, and that gene count would correlate roughly with organismal complexity. None of this turned out to be right. We have around 19,000-20,000 protein-coding genes, which is fewer than rice and about the same as a roundworm. The "gene for X" model collapsed almost immediately for most complex traits. GWAS, when it scaled up after 2007, found that complex traits and common diseases involve hundreds to thousands of variants of small effect, with no single gene doing the work the framework had expected. Most of the genome is noncoding, and the ENCODE project's 2012 claim that 80% of the genome is "functional" was overstated and contested but it broke the simple coding-versus-junk dichotomy. The non-coding genome is doing enormous regulatory work that wasn't appreciated. This is the development that, more than any other, exposed the limits of gene-centric biology in a way the field couldn't ignore, and it's the fact base behind the entire post-genocentric conversation we've been having. The technical work was enormously successful; the conceptual frame the work was sold under was substantially wrong.

The microbiome revolution. In 2000 the microbiome was a backwater of microbiology — interesting if you were a digestive specialist, but not a major topic in mainstream biology. By 2025 it's clear that the human body is a holobiont, that we carry roughly as many microbial cells as human cells (the old 10:1 figure was wrong but the rough parity is real), that the microbiome is implicated in immunity, metabolism, mood, neurodegeneration, autoimmune disease, allergy, and probably much else, and that we've been substantially miscounting what an organism is. The "human" was always a community. This connects directly to what we discussed — Hoffmeyer's biosemiotic picture handles holobionts naturally because semiosis runs across the species boundaries; the gene-centric picture had to be substantially revised to absorb them. The microbiome turn is one of the clearest cases of the "constituent rather than ornamental" pattern. What looked like ecological background turned out to be ontological foreground.

Epigenetics and transgenerational inheritance. This is one of the most contested and conceptually important developments. The claim, which would have been heretical in 2000, is that environmental exposures during one's life can produce heritable changes in gene expression (via DNA methylation, histone modification, small RNAs) that persist across generations without any change to the DNA sequence itself. The strong version of transgenerational epigenetic inheritance in mammals remains contested empirically — there are real cases (Dutch Hunger Winter cohort effects, some animal models, paternal stress effects on offspring) but the magnitude and durability across multiple generations is debated. What's no longer contested is that gene expression is profoundly shaped by environment through epigenetic mechanisms, that early-life experiences have lasting epigenomic signatures, and that the strict Weismann barrier between germline and soma is leakier than the textbook story allowed. This is exactly the development that vindicates niche construction and developmental systems theory empirically, and it's reshaping evolutionary biology in slow motion.

CRISPR. The discovery that bacterial immune systems use CRISPR-Cas to recognize and cut viral DNA, and that this could be retooled into a programmable genome editor, is probably the single biggest technical revolution in biology of the period. From the first papers in 2012 (Doudna, Charpentier, Zhang and others) to therapeutic applications and the first FDA-approved CRISPR therapy in 2023 took just over a decade. This is genuine technical revolution rather than conceptual revolution — it didn't change what we think life is, it changed what we can do to it. But the speed of deployment, the fact that the system was hiding in plain sight in bacterial genomes for decades before anyone realized what it was, and the cascade of derivative technologies (base editing, prime editing, gene drives) make it the defining technical achievement of the period.

Single-cell sequencing and the cell atlas project. By 2010 it was becoming possible to sequence the transcriptomes of individual cells; by 2020 this was routine at scale, with millions of cells from single experiments. The Human Cell Atlas project, started in 2016, aims to produce a complete reference of cell types and states in the human body. What this has revealed is that "cell type" was a much messier category than the textbook taxonomy suggested. Cells exist in continuous spaces of states rather than discrete types, transitions between states are gradual, and the diversity within tissues is enormous. We're discovering that organs we thought we understood contain cell populations nobody had identified before, sometimes performing functions nobody knew were happening. The Cell Atlas and related projects (HuBMAP, Human Tumor Atlas Network) are building the kind of comprehensive descriptive infrastructure that anatomy did in the 19th century, at a much finer grain.

Protein structure prediction by AlphaFold. In 2020-2021 AlphaFold2 essentially solved the protein folding problem that had been a grand challenge for 50 years. Not solved in the sense of explaining how proteins fold, but solved in the sense of being able to predict structure from sequence with accuracy comparable to experimental determination. The AlphaFold Protein Structure Database now contains predictions for essentially every protein in every sequenced organism — over 200 million structures. This is a transformation in what can be done, not a transformation in what we understand mechanistically, but the scale of the change in working biology is hard to overstate. Every structural biology question now starts with "let me check AlphaFold."

Optogenetics and the techniques revolution in neuroscience. The development of optogenetics around 2005 (Deisseroth, Boyden, others) — using light to control neurons that have been engineered to express light-sensitive ion channels — gave neuroscience the ability to do causal experiments on specific cell populations in awake behaving animals. This sounds technical but it transformed what neuroscience could ask. Combined with calcium imaging (which lets you watch the activity of thousands of neurons simultaneously) and large-scale electrode arrays, neuroscience has moved from observing correlations to manipulating circuits in real time. The functional architecture of neural circuits is being mapped at an unprecedented level. The hard problem of consciousness is no closer to solved — those gains haven't translated into understanding why any of this is accompanied by experience — but the empirical understanding of what neural circuits do has advanced enormously.

The connectome. The first complete mapping of a nervous system at synaptic resolution was C. elegans (302 neurons), completed in 1986. By 2024 we had the complete connectome of the adult fruit fly brain (about 140,000 neurons, 50 million synapses) — the FlyWire project. The mouse connectome is in progress. The human connectome at synaptic resolution is still far away, but mesoscale connectomics (regional connectivity) has produced detailed maps that didn't exist before. We can now ask questions about neural architecture as a topological object that were unanswerable a generation ago.

Evo-devo and the surprising conservation of developmental toolkits. The discovery, primarily across the 1990s and 2000s, that fundamentally similar genetic toolkits underlie development across enormously different animals — that the genes specifying body plans in flies and humans are deeply homologous, that Hox genes and signaling pathways are conserved across bilaterians — overturned the expectation that different body plans would require different developmental machinery. The same toolkit gets redeployed combinatorially. This connects to the questions we've been asking: organismal form isn't specified by genome size or gene count but by how a relatively conserved toolkit is regulated and deployed in development. The information is in the regulation, not the parts list.

Mike Levin's bioelectricity work. We discussed this earlier, so I'll just mark the period: from roughly 2000 onward, Levin's lab has built a body of work showing that bioelectric signaling across cells specifies anatomical patterns, that target morphologies are stored in bioelectric circuits not just in genes, that regenerative capacity can be modulated by manipulating bioelectric state, and that cells removed from their normal context (xenobots, anthrobots) self-organize in ways that suggest "cognition" extends to non-neural systems. This work is heterodox but increasingly accepted on the empirical findings if not on the conceptual framing. It's one of the most paradigm-pressing programs in current biology.

The reality of horizontal gene transfer in eukaryotes. We knew bacteria did horizontal gene transfer; what's emerged across the 2000s and 2010s is that eukaryotes do it more than was thought. Some of the most striking cases involve insects acquiring genes from bacteria, plants acquiring genes from other plants, and aphids acquiring carotenoid biosynthesis genes from fungi. The clean tree-of-life picture, with vertical inheritance dominating, is partly a network rather than a tree, especially deeper in evolutionary time.

The surprising findings about ancient DNA and human prehistory. Starting around 2010 with the Neanderthal genome, ancient DNA work has rewritten human prehistory. Most non-Africans carry 1-4% Neanderthal DNA from interbreeding events. Denisovans, an entirely unknown human lineage, were discovered from a finger bone in Siberia in 2010 and turn out to have contributed DNA to modern Asian populations, especially Tibetans (whose high-altitude adaptation gene EPAS1 came from Denisovans). Most of the major movements of human populations across Eurasia have been remapped through ancient DNA. This is a case where the technology (ancient DNA sequencing) made tractable questions that were previously settled by speculation, and the answers turned out to be more complicated than expected.

The replication crisis and what it revealed. Starting around 2011-2015, systematic attempts to replicate published findings in psychology and biomedical research found that very large fractions failed — somewhere between a third and half of major published findings in psychology, large fractions of preclinical cancer research findings. This isn't a discovery in biology in the usual sense, but it's a discovery about biology as a knowledge-producing enterprise. The implications are still being absorbed: many "established" findings turned out not to be, statistical practices need reform, publication incentives produce systematic distortions. This is closely related to what we discussed about the gap between productivity and well-foundedness.

Mitochondria and metabolism reconsidered.
