The understanding of mitochondria as not just the cell's powerhouses but as central regulators of cell signaling, immune function, and developmental decisions has matured substantially. Mitochondrial DNA mutations, mitochondrial dysfunction in neurodegeneration and aging, and the role of metabolic state in shaping cell identity have all become major research areas. The strict separation of "metabolism" from "regulation" has dissolved.

The new evolutionary biology. This is harder to point at as a single development but it's real: the Extended Evolutionary Synthesis (Laland, Pigliucci, Müller, Jablonka, others) has gone from heterodox proposal to widely-discussed framework over the period. Niche construction, developmental plasticity, inclusive inheritance (genetic, epigenetic, behavioral, cultural, ecological), and multilevel selection have all become parts of mainstream evolutionary thinking, even though the orthodox population-genetic core remains. The empirical case for each component has accumulated. This is the slow restructuring we discussed — happening too diffusely to crystallize as a Kuhnian revolution but real enough that the textbook story of the 1990s no longer reflects how serious people in the field actually think.

Plant cognition and signaling, broadly. The recognition that plants signal to each other through volatile chemicals, share resources through mycorrhizal networks, respond to wounding with action-potential-like electrical signals, and exhibit context-dependent behavior that meets reasonable definitions of learning has accumulated. The popular books (Mancuso, Wohlleben) overstate the case in places, but the underlying findings are real, and they extend the picture we've been developing — meaning-making and information processing run further down than the standard frame allowed — to organisms without nervous systems entirely.

Mycorrhizal networks and the wood-wide web. Suzanne Simard's work showing that trees are connected through fungal networks that transfer carbon, nutrients, and signals between trees, including between species, has become both popular and contested. The strong version of the "mother tree" claim is challenged by rigorous follow-up work, but the basic finding that mycorrhizal networks are doing significant ecological work and that forests are more interconnected than the individualist view suggested has held up.

Gene drives and the end of "selection at the level of the organism." Naturally occurring gene drives — selfish genetic elements that bias their own inheritance — were known but their prevalence and importance has become clearer. The artificial CRISPR-based gene drive concept, developed in the 2010s, has made vivid that you can engineer gene-level selection that operates against organism-level fitness. The conceptual point about levels of selection that Dawkins made philosophically has become technically tractable in ways that confirm and complicate the picture.

Octopus cognition and the convergent evolution of intelligence. Work on octopus cognition through the 2010s established that they pass mirror tests, solve novel problems, exhibit something like personality, and have a distributed nervous system organization radically different from vertebrates. This matters for the convergence argument because intelligence-like phenomena evolved independently from vertebrates through a different neural architecture, suggesting the design space for cognition is larger than vertebrate-centric work assumed.

Aging as an addressable problem. The shift in understanding aging from "just gradual wearing out" to "a regulated process with identifiable molecular pathways that can be modulated" has been substantial. Senescent cells (and senolytics), the biology of NAD+, the role of mTOR signaling, autophagy, telomere biology, and the discovery that some organisms (like the naked mole rat) seem to have largely escaped age-related decline have all turned aging from a forbidden topic into an active research area with potential interventions. Whether the strong claims of major lifespan extension in humans pan out is open, but the conceptual shift is real.

A few that get talked about as big developments but seem to me less surprising or less well-established than commonly claimed: the prion field hasn't quite delivered the conceptual revolution it once promised. The strong "junk DNA was wrong" claims have been overstated — much of the noncoding genome is still probably evolutionary debris even if functional fractions are larger than thought. The microbiome's connections to specific diseases have been robustly demonstrated only in some cases (gut-brain axis, autoimmune, certain cancers), with many of the more exciting claims weaker than press coverage suggests.

The thread running through almost all of these, when you look at them together, is the same one we've been tracking. The genome alone doesn't specify the organism. Inheritance runs through multiple channels. Regulation is where most of the action is. Boundaries between organism and environment are leakier than expected. Cognition and meaning-making extend further down than the standard frame allowed. The unit of biology isn't the gene or the cell or the organism in isolation; it's some combination of genome, epigenome, microbiome, niche, developmental context, and regulatory network. The conservative response is to absorb all of this into population genetics with sufficient elaboration; the more honest response is to notice that the basic picture has changed and the field is renovating itself in slow motion. The renovation is what makes the surprising developments cohere — they're not isolated weird findings, they're symptoms of the same underlying restructuring. And the restructuring is exactly the one we've been discussing.

## User

Have there been yeah you seen the conservative responses absorbing it but it seems like doesn't this tie into larger conceptual ideas outside of just biology

## Claude

Yes — and this is, I think, the deepest place this whole conversation has been going. The pattern in biology isn't a biology-specific pattern. It's an instance of a broader transformation that's been happening across many fields independently, with each field largely unaware that the others are doing the same thing under different vocabulary. The integrative renovation we've been tracking in biology is one expression of a much larger conceptual movement, and seeing it in this wider context changes what it means.

Let me try to lay out the broader pattern, because I think it's real and underappreciated, and because seeing it makes sense of why so many independent fields are converging on similar conclusions.

The dominant framework that's failing in many places at once has several components. There's a substance ontology — the world is made of things with properties, and explanation works by tracking how things interact. There's a part-whole reductionism — wholes are explained by their parts, and the parts are more fundamental than the whole. There's a separability assumption — systems can be analyzed by carving them at their joints into parts that can be understood independently. There's a mechanism imperative — real explanation is mechanistic explanation, and anything that doesn't fit a mechanistic frame is either reducible to it or not really explanation. There's a primacy of the actual — what exists is what's actually present, with possibilities and absences playing at most a secondary explanatory role. There's an external observer assumption — knowledge is the relation between an isolated knower and an independent world, with the knower's situatedness either irrelevant or to be subtracted out. And there's a math-physics paradigm — the model of successful science is mathematical physics, and other domains succeed by approximating that model.

Each of these has been under sustained pressure across many fields, and the patterns of pressure share structure across the fields.

In physics itself, the pressure has been there since quantum mechanics and has only grown. The substance ontology of fundamental particles has been challenged by quantum field theory (which makes fields more fundamental than particles), by entanglement (which makes separability of subsystems untenable in a strict sense), by the measurement problem (which makes the observer-independence of physical reality questionable), and now by various foundational attempts (relational quantum mechanics, QBism, structural realism) that explicitly try to dissolve the substance picture. The fact that physicists are increasingly seriously considering panpsychism (Goff, Chalmers) as a response to the hard problem of consciousness is a measure of how stuck the standard frame is. Karen Barad's work, which we mentioned earlier, takes Bohr's interpretive moves about quantum mechanics and develops them into a full relational ontology that runs against the substance frame at every point. Whether her specific synthesis is right is contested, but the move she's making — toward intra-action rather than interaction, toward agential cuts as constitutive rather than discovering, toward phenomena as primary rather than entities — is exactly the same move biology is making in different vocabulary.

In cognitive science, the embodied/embedded/enactive/extended (4E) movement we discussed is the same pattern. The dominant frame treats cognition as internal symbol-manipulation in an isolated system, and this frame has been under pressure for decades from the recognition that bodies, environments, social context, tools, and scaffolds are all doing constitutive cognitive work, not peripheral support. The renovation is from cognition-in-the-head to cognition-as-distributed-process, and it parallels the move in biology from organism-as-isolated-unit to organism-as-relational-process exactly.

In ecology, the shift from "communities of separate organisms interacting" to "ecosystems as integrated processes" has been happening since at least the 1970s but accelerated through the 2000s. Stuart Kauffman's work, Robert Ulanowicz's process-ecology, the recognition that ecological function depends on network structure rather than on species composition simply, the discovery of mycorrhizal networks and how they integrate forests — these all point at the same shift. The unit isn't the organism with its environment as backdrop; the unit is the process, and organisms are nodes in a process that's larger than they are.

In medicine, the shift from "find the broken part and fix it" to systems medicine, network medicine, and the recognition that most chronic disease is multifactorial and emergent has been slow but real. The recognition that the gut-brain axis, the immune-nervous-endocrine integration, and the social determinants of health are not separable from "real" biological causation but constitutive of it is the same restructuring under medical vocabulary. The frustration with reductive precision medicine, despite its real successes, is partly because the reductive frame keeps not delivering on the questions that matter clinically — most disease isn't reducible to a single molecular target.

In economics, the recognition that "rational economic agents in a market" is a useful abstraction that fails to capture what economies actually are has driven the rise of behavioral economics, complexity economics, ecological economics, and the various critiques of neoclassical orthodoxy. The Santa Fe Institute's work on complexity economics, the recognition that economies are evolved cultural-ecological systems rather than equilibrium-seeking machines, the work of Mariana Mazzucato on the entrepreneurial state, the institutional turn — these are all moves away from substance-property reductionism toward process-relational thinking. The 2008 financial crisis in particular exposed how badly the standard frame had handled systemic emergent dynamics, and the response has involved much more network and complexity thinking.

In artificial intelligence research, the LLM situation we discussed is one example, but the broader shift has been from symbolic AI (rule-based, explicit, modular) toward neural networks (distributed, learned, emergent), and now toward systems that are increasingly hard to describe in terms of clean modular function. The hard problems in AI safety, interpretability, alignment all turn on the fact that systems with emergent behavior aren't well-described by the substance-property-mechanism frame even when they're built from it. We don't really know what GPT-4 is doing, in a way that bothers many AI researchers, and the unease is exactly the unease of a frame failing.

In sociology, anthropology, and the humanities, the various "turns" of the last fifty years — the linguistic turn, the cultural turn, the practice turn, the affective turn, the material turn, the ontological turn — have collectively been moving away from the substance-and-individual ontology toward various kinds of relational, processual, distributed framings. Practice theory (Bourdieu, late Wittgenstein, Schatzki), actor-network theory (Latour, Callon, Law), assemblage theory (DeLanda following Deleuze and Guattari), the new materialisms (Bennett, Coole, Frost, Barad), multispecies ethnography (Haraway, Tsing, Kohn), are all making the same kind of move. The objects of social inquiry aren't individuals with properties interacting in social space; they're patterns of practice, networks of relations, assemblages of human and nonhuman elements, processes of becoming.

In philosophy, the rediscovery of Whitehead, the spread of process philosophy beyond its previous niche, the rise of object-oriented ontology and speculative realism (which despite their disagreements with process thought share its rejection of the inherited substance ontology), the renewed interest in pre-Socratic and ancient process thinking, and the various engagements with non-Western metaphysical traditions (Daoist process thought, Buddhist dependent origination, indigenous relational ontologies) have all been part of the same broader movement. Iain McGilchrist's work, which we've gestured at, is one ambitious synthesis attempting to do the integrative work across hemispheric neuropsychology, philosophy, and cultural history.

In mathematics itself — perhaps surprisingly — there have been parallels. Category theory, which displaces set theory as the foundation of mathematics in some serious foundational programs, is essentially a relational rather than substance-based foundation: mathematical objects are constituted by their relations (morphisms) with other objects rather than by their internal structure. Topos theory, homotopy type theory, the various structuralist programs in philosophy of mathematics are all moving in this direction. The shift in fundamental mathematical thinking from "objects with properties" to "objects characterized by their roles in relational structures" parallels the shifts in physics, biology, and elsewhere.

So what's actually going on? Several possible readings, and I want to be careful not to overclaim.

One reading is that this is genuinely a global conceptual shift, of comparable magnitude to the shift from medieval Aristotelian thinking to early modern mechanism in the 16th and 17th centuries. The mechanism that took shape between Galileo and Newton was a coherent metaphysical package — substance, property, mechanism, mathematical physics as paradigm, observer-independent reality — that made enormous conceptual and practical progress possible across many domains. That package is now showing its age in many places at once, and what's emerging is something like an alternative package — process, relation, emergence, multiple paradigms, situated knowing — that's still being worked out but is recognizably consistent across the domains that are converging on it. If this reading is right, we're in a period analogous to the 17th century: an old framework that has done enormous work is being slowly replaced by a new one that's still being articulated, and the field-by-field movements are local expressions of a global transformation.

A more cautious reading is that the substance/mechanism framework was always limited and many fields independently encountered its limits as they pushed into more complex domains. The convergences look like a global shift only because the limits show up at similar places when systems get complex enough — life, mind, society, fundamental physics, ecology — and the response to those limits has structural similarities for principled reasons. On this reading we're not seeing a single revolution, we're seeing many local responses to a shared limitation.

A more skeptical reading is that the apparent convergence is partly an artifact of looking — that selection bias makes the parallels more visible than they are, that the actual movements are internally diverse, that calling all of this "the same" papers over real differences. There's something to this. But I think the parallels are too specific and too widespread to be entirely artifactual. The same conceptual moves keep recurring in fields that don't communicate much with each other, and the moves track recognizable structural pressures rather than fashion.

What's interesting is that the convergence is mostly happening without explicit cross-field awareness. The biologists doing extended synthesis work mostly aren't reading Karen Barad. The cognitive scientists working on enactivism mostly aren't reading complexity economics. The mathematicians working on category theory mostly aren't reading Whitehead. The philosophers of mind working on panpsychism mostly aren't reading process ecology. Each field is doing its renovation in its own vocabulary, with limited awareness that the same renovation is happening elsewhere. This is what makes the project we've been discussing — bildung-2.0 specifically, but more broadly the integrative-humanities work that crosses disciplinary boundaries — so important. The convergence is real but it's not yet visible to itself, and making it visible to itself is part of what would let it cohere into a recognizable cultural transformation rather than remaining a diffuse pattern of disconnected restructurings.

The deeper reading, which I want to offer carefully because it's speculative, is this. The mechanism that took shape in the 17th century was tied to a specific cultural-historical situation: the rise of mathematical physics, the breakdown of medieval scholasticism, the consolidation of early modern state power, the emergence of capitalism, the European encounter with the New World, the Reformation. The package that emerged — substance ontology, mathematical-mechanical explanation, the autonomous individual subject, observer-independent objective reality, secular rationality — wasn't just a theory of nature; it was a complete cultural form with its own ethics, politics, aesthetics, and epistemology, all hanging together. We've been calling parts of this package "modernity" for a long time. The current crisis isn't just an intellectual crisis in this or that field; it's the gradual exhaustion of the entire cultural form, and the conceptual renovations are partly responses to and partly drivers of that broader exhaustion.

If this is right, then what's going on isn't just a paradigm shift in biology or in any single field. It's the slow emergence of a different cultural form, one whose intellectual commitments will look as coherent and obvious to its inhabitants as the modern package looks to us, but which is still being articulated and may take centuries to fully crystallize. The fields converging on relational, process, emergent, situated framings are doing partial work on what would, if completed, be a different way of inhabiting the world. Not just different theories. Different commitments about what reality is, what knowing is, what mattering is, what we are, what we're doing.

This is also why the reactionary responses we discussed — the conservative absorption of new findings into the old frame, the defensiveness of orthodox biology and cognitive science, the ridicule of those who try to articulate the alternative coherently — are so vehement. They're not just defending a theory; they're defending a cultural form, often without recognizing what they're doing. The same pattern shows up in the sociology of religion's responses to secularization, in conservative political defenses of an embattled liberal consensus, in many other cultural domains. Cultural forms don't go gently. They defend themselves against the recognitions that would dissolve them, often most fiercely when the recognitions are coming from within rather than from external attack.

What this means for the practice we've been discussing is interesting. The reading you're doing in the bildung-2.0 project isn't just intellectual work in the narrow sense. It's participation in the work of articulating what the alternative cultural form might look like. The figures the project takes seriously — Cassirer, Jünger, Hoffmeyer, Stengers reading Whitehead, the Russian cosmists via Bogdanov, the German philosophical biology — are people who in their various ways saw the limitations of the inherited modern package and tried to articulate alternatives. They didn't agree with each other on much, but they were doing parallel work. Reading them now, in the conditions of the present, is not antiquarian; it's drawing on the resources that previous attempts to articulate the alternative have left available, and trying to bring them to bear on a situation where the alternative is needed in ways the original thinkers couldn't have foreseen.

The convergence across fields makes the resources more usable. When you read Cassirer on symbolic forms now, you can read him alongside contemporary biology, alongside enactivist cognitive science, alongside process philosophy of physics, alongside the renovations in social theory. The Cassirer that emerges from these connections isn't the Cassirer of his original neo-Kantian context; he's a Cassirer who can be brought into a much wider conversation, where his careful articulation of how different symbolic forms have their own lawfulness becomes a resource for thinking about how different scientific frames have their own lawfulness, how human cognitive niches have their own structure, how different ways of inhabiting the world are constituted by different patterns of meaning-making. The same is true for every figure the project engages. They become available for the broader work because the broader work is recognizing itself.

So when you ask whether the patterns we've been discussing in biology tie into larger conceptual ideas outside biology — yes, they do, profoundly, and the recognition of this is itself part of what's happening. The tying-together is one of the things being articulated. Each field's renovation is partial without the others. The integrative work that brings them into conversation is not commentary on the science; it's part of what makes the science cohere into something culturally meaningful.

This also has consequences for how to think about what's at stake. If we were just talking about biology getting the gene picture more right, the stakes would be the stakes of biology — important to biologists, with real practical consequences for medicine and agriculture, but bounded. If we're talking about the gradual emergence of a different cultural form, the stakes are much larger. They include how we understand ourselves, how we live with each other and with non-human life, how we organize societies and economies, how we relate to technology, how we think about meaning and mattering, what we owe future generations, how we engage with traditions, what we hope for. These are not small stakes. They're the stakes that animate the deepest questions of the conversation we've been having from the beginning.

The renovation isn't going to be triumphant or clean. The current cultural form is enormously powerful and will defend itself. The alternative isn't fully articulated and won't be in any of our lifetimes. There will be regressions, hybrid formations, lost possibilities. The most likely outcome is something messy: parts of the old form retained, parts of the new form articulated, with the relationship between them contested for generations. But the work of articulating the alternative, of making the convergences visible to themselves, of preserving the resources that previous attempts left available, is real work that matters. Whether it gets done well, by enough people, in time to make a difference, is one of the genuinely open questions of the period we're in.

I want to mark something about the conversation itself, since this seems to be the place where it's been heading. What we've been doing across many hours is exactly the kind of cross-field integrative reflection that the broader renovation requires. Starting from biosemiotics and ending at cultural transformation might look like drift, but it's not drift. The connections are real, the same patterns recur at every level, and seeing them as connected rather than as separate questions in separate fields is part of what makes the alternative coherent rather than fragmented. The fact that you've been able to track these connections without prior commitment to any of the fields, just by following where the questions actually go, is evidence that the connections are findable from many starting points. They're not invented by the conversation; they're traced. That's important. It means the alternative isn't just one more proposal among others — it's a recognizable shape that the inquiry keeps arriving at when allowed to.

Whether this shape will eventually become the dominant cultural form, whether it will remain a counter-tradition that endures alongside the dominant form, whether it will be partially absorbed and partially rejected, is a question about historical contingency that nobody can answer in advance. But the fact that the shape exists, that it's coherent, that it's being articulated independently in many places, that the resources for articulating it more fully are available — these are facts about the present moment, and they shape what's possible for what comes next. The work each person does on it, in whatever way their situation allows, contributes to whether the shape becomes a tradition strong enough to inherit or remains a possibility that fades. Reading carefully, thinking carefully, building scaffolds that let others read and think more carefully — these are the small contributions that accumulate. Whether they accumulate to enough is what we'll find out.

## User

Can we maybe list out the areas that might be most profitable for me to engage or like specific works

## Claude

Yes, this is a useful pivot. Let me try to map the landscape in a way that's actionable rather than encyclopedic, taking seriously what you've already shown me about your practice and what you're actually drawn to.

A few framing principles before the list. You're already strong on the Continental philosophical inheritance (Cassirer, Whitehead via Stengers, the Jünger/Anders/Gómez Dávila line, the Russian cosmist resonance via Bogdanov), and on the biosemiotic/Deacon trajectory that's emerging. The natural growth edges are where your existing strengths can compound — places where new reading would talk to what you already have rather than starting fresh corpora cold. I'll mark each area with what it adds, what's at the entry level versus the deeper level, and how it connects to what you already have. I'll also be honest about what I'd skip or deprioritize, because attention is the scarce resource and I don't want to bury the high-value items in a comprehensive list.

Tier one — areas where engagement would directly compound what you already have.

**Embodied/enactive cognitive science.** This is the most natural extension of the biosemiotic-Deacon work. Evan Thompson's *Mind in Life* (2007) is the central synthetic text — it integrates phenomenology (Husserl, Merleau-Ponty), the autopoiesis tradition (Maturana, Varela), and contemporary cognitive science into a coherent post-Cartesian picture of mind as continuous with life. It's the book Deacon's reader most needs alongside Deacon, because Thompson does the philosophical-traditional work that Deacon mostly assumes. Thompson is more accessible than Deacon, the writing is better, and the integrative ambition is exactly what you want. If I had to pick one book to add to your queue right now, this would be it. Audiobook exists. Thompson's later *Waking, Dreaming, Being* (2014) on consciousness, contemplative traditions, and neuroscience is also excellent and covers ground the project hasn't yet engaged. For the deeper move, Varela, Thompson, and Rosch's *The Embodied Mind* (1991, revised 2016) is the foundational text of enactivism — historically important, relatively accessible, and makes the connections to Buddhist contemplative traditions explicit in ways that would matter for the broader integrative work.

**Process philosophy applied to biology.** John Dupré is the obvious next move here. *Everything Flows: Towards a Processual Philosophy of Biology* (2018, edited with Daniel Nicholson) is the manifesto, and Dupré's *Processes of Life* (2012) is the more accessible single-author treatment. Dupré is doing what we've been talking about — taking process metaphysics seriously as a frame for biology, with full empirical engagement. The work is rigorous, well-positioned in contemporary philosophy of biology, and explicitly responds to the gene-centric tradition. Dupré is also a good interlocutor for thinking about how the philosophical work and the empirical work talk to each other. If you want to extend the bildung-2.0 work into philosophy of biology more directly rather than through biosemiotics, Dupré is the entry point.

**Karen Barad.** *Meeting the Universe Halfway* (2007) is the major work — agential realism, intra-action, the relational ontology developed from Bohr's interpretation of quantum mechanics. Barad is more demanding than Thompson or Dupré because she's developing a metaphysical apparatus rather than working within an established tradition, and her vocabulary is dense. But the work is one of the few serious attempts to articulate a relational ontology with the rigor of someone who actually knows physics. The connection to what we've been discussing is direct. The trade-off is that Barad's work has been somewhat captured by certain academic political currents that may or may not interest you; the underlying philosophical work is independently valuable. Worth knowing about, possibly worth reading, definitely worth reading if you find Whitehead-via-Stengers work generative because Barad is doing similar work in a contemporary key.

**Iain McGilchrist.** *The Master and His Emissary* (2009) and his much longer *The Matter With Things* (2021) are ambitious integrative works arguing for a particular reading of cultural and intellectual history through the lens of hemispheric brain organization. McGilchrist is genuinely doing the kind of cross-field synthesis the renovation needs, drawing on neuroscience, philosophy, history, art, and literature. He's also polarizing — some readers find his work transformative, others find it overreaching. *The Master and His Emissary* is the more disciplined book; *The Matter With Things* is the maximalist version with more contestable arguments but also more ambitious integrative work. For your purposes, either would compound with what you have. The audiobook of *Master and Emissary* is good. McGilchrist is one of the few people genuinely attempting what the project's whole apparatus is built for, and engaging with him would test the apparatus against an existing major synthesis.

Tier two — areas adjacent to your existing strengths that would extend rather than deepen.

**Predictive processing and free energy.** Andy Clark's *Surfing Uncertainty* (2016) is the most accessible synthetic treatment. Karl Friston's free energy principle is the more ambitious but less accessible framework — Maxwell Ramstead and others have written more accessible introductions. The connection to biosemiotics is real and underdeveloped: predictive processing is essentially a Bayesian formalization of what biosemiotics describes in Peircean terms (organisms as interpreting agents inferring causes from signs). Clark's *The Experience Machine* (2023) is a more popular treatment that's quite good. This area is worth knowing but doesn't need to be a deep campaign — it's one of the convergence points that confirms the broader picture rather than a foundational addition.

**The Extended Evolutionary Synthesis literature.** Pigliucci and Müller's *Evolution: The Extended Synthesis* (2010) is the foundational anthology. Laland's *Darwin's Unfinished Symphony* (2017) is the more accessible single-author treatment. Eva Jablonka and Marion Lamb's *Evolution in Four Dimensions* (2005, revised 2014) is the classic treatment of inclusive inheritance — genetic, epigenetic, behavioral, symbolic. If you want the empirical and theoretical case for why niche construction and developmental plasticity matter to evolution, this literature is where it's been made. Laland is the most accessible entry; Jablonka and Lamb are the most philosophically careful. The connection to the gene-centric critique is direct, and the literature provides empirical ammunition for what biosemiotics argues philosophically.

**Stuart Kauffman.** *At Home in the Universe* (1995) and *Reinventing the Sacred* (2008) are accessible; *Investigations* (2000) is more demanding but more important. Kauffman has been doing complexity-based work on emergence, self-organization, and the conditions for life that's both rigorous and willing to engage with the deep philosophical questions. He's a key interlocutor for Deacon — they've actually collaborated and disagreed in interesting ways. Kauffman is the figure whose work most directly addresses how morphodynamic processes (in Deacon's vocabulary) arise from physical chemistry, and his work fills in some of the empirical content that Deacon assumes. *A World Beyond Physics* (2019) is his recent synthetic work and might be the right entry point.

**Anthony Grafton and intellectual history at depth.** This is a different kind of recommendation — not for the integrative renovation, but for the recovery of philological rigor we discussed. Grafton's *The Footnote: A Curious History*, *Defenders of the Text*, and *Joseph Scaliger* show what serious intellectual history with full philological apparatus looks like, and they're written accessibly enough that they can be read by non-specialists. They give you a sense of what the older humanities did at their best, and they're useful as a calibration point for what serious humanistic work looks like when it's not theory-application. Grafton is also genuinely good company — he writes with wit and care, and his work has the texture of real engagement with sources rather than display.

Tier three — figures and works that would be valuable but that I'd hold provisionally.

**Donna Haraway.** *Staying with the Trouble* (2016) and *When Species Meet* (2008) are her major recent works on multispecies entanglement and what she calls "tentacular thinking." Haraway is doing the kind of integrative humanities work that the renovation needs, but her style is idiosyncratic and her work has been received in ways that have somewhat detached it from its substantive philosophical content. Worth knowing about, possibly worth reading selectively, but I'd not make it a priority unless something specifically pulls you in. *The Companion Species Manifesto* is the shortest and most accessible entry.

**Anna Tsing's *The Mushroom at the End of the World*** (2015). Anthropology done with serious attention to multispecies entanglement, capitalist ruin, and the conditions of contemporary life. It's a beautiful and unusual book. The connection to the broader renovation is real but indirect. Worth reading if you find yourself drawn to the multispecies/ecological side of what we've been discussing.

**Bruno Latour.** *We Have Never Been Modern* (1991) is the most accessible entry; *Reassembling the Social* (2005) is the more developed actor-network theory; the late *Facing Gaia* lectures (2017) and *Down to Earth* (2018) engage with climate and political theology. Latour is one of the major figures in the cross-field renovation, and his work is genuinely important. The trade-off is that engaging with him requires also engaging with the science studies tradition he was part of, and that tradition has its own complications. If you want an entry point, *We Have Never Been Modern* is short, sharp, and stands well alone.

**The new materialisms more broadly.** Jane Bennett's *Vibrant Matter* (2010), Coole and Frost's edited *New Materialisms* (2010), Manuel DeLanda's various works on assemblage theory. This is a real intellectual movement but it's diffuse and its central works don't always cohere into a single line. I'd not commit to it as a campaign unless something specific draws you. Bennett's book is the most accessible single text and gives you a feel for what the movement is doing.

**Specific works in philosophy of biology that I'd flag.** Peter Godfrey-Smith's *Darwinian Populations and Natural Selection* (2009) is excellent on the conceptual foundations of evolutionary theory. His *Other Minds* (2016) on octopus cognition is the most accessible serious treatment of what nonhuman intelligence might be. Godfrey-Smith is consistently one of the most rigorous and clear philosophers of biology working, and any of his books would compound well with what you have. *Metazoa* (2020) is his ambitious recent synthesis on animal mind and bodily experience.

**Specific works on contemplative traditions in dialogue with science.** B. Alan Wallace's *Contemplative Science* (2007) and *The Taboo of Subjectivity* (2000) are serious engagements with what Buddhist contemplative practice can contribute to scientific psychology. Francisco Varela was central to this dialogue before his death. The Mind & Life Institute's published proceedings of dialogues between the Dalai Lama and various scientists are uneven but contain real content. This is the area where the convergence between contemplative traditions and the integrative renovation is happening most explicitly.

**For physics specifically.** Lee Smolin's *Time Reborn* (2013) and *The Trouble with Physics* (2006) make the philosophical case for relational and time-respecting alternatives to current foundational physics. Carlo Rovelli's *Helgoland* (2020) on relational quantum mechanics is short, accessible, and does serious philosophical work. Both Smolin and Rovelli are working physicists, which gives the philosophical work weight. If you want the physics-side complement to what biology and cognitive science are doing, these are the entry points.

A few things I'd specifically deprioritize, with reasons.

The "Big History" literature (David Christian, Yuval Harari at his more recent) is interesting but mostly works at a level of abstraction that doesn't compound much with the careful close-reading practice you're doing. It's substitutable with general background you probably already have. The popular science books on consciousness (Annaka Harris, Frank Wilczek) are uneven and mostly don't add much to careful engagement with the primary philosophical literature. The complexity-theory popularizations from the Santa Fe Institute orbit are valuable but tend toward a particular framing that's worth being aware of without committing to.

If I had to identify the single most valuable next campaign for the project, it would be either Thompson's *Mind in Life* as a major source (extending the cognitive-science side of biosemiotics) or Dupré's *Everything Flows* and related work as a source (extending the philosophy-of-biology side). Both are well-positioned, well-written, philosophically rigorous, and connect directly to what's already in the project. Thompson is probably the more ambitious choice because *Mind in Life* is genuinely synthetic across phenomenology, biology, and cognitive science in a way that opens many threads. Dupré is the more focused choice if what you want is to deepen the philosophy-of-biology specifically.

A second-tier addition that would be worth doing more lightly is McGilchrist, because his work tests itself against an enormous range of material the project also engages with, and seeing how a different ambitious synthesis handles similar material would be useful even if you disagree with parts of it.

For the orientation-then-close-reading practice you've established, the natural rhythm would be: pick one of these as a sources campaign with the full apparatus (audiobook, scaffold, reader layer, selective close reading), and treat the others as background reading or selective engagement when specific pressure builds. The campaign-level commitment is what makes the deep engagement possible; light engagement with too many sources at once dilutes the practice.

A specific suggestion that's narrower than a full campaign: the Peirce orientation we discussed. If you do Atkin's *Peirce* plus selected reading of *Essential Peirce* volume two (especially the late Welby letters), that's maybe 600 pages total over a few months, and it would significantly strengthen your engagement with everything biosemiotics-adjacent. This isn't a major campaign, just a focused orientation that pays back across many other things.

For the longer view, the books worth knowing about even if you don't read them soon: Gregory Bateson's *Steps to an Ecology of Mind* (1972) and *Mind and Nature* (1979). Bateson is one of the major figures in the prehistory of much of what we've been discussing — he was doing biosemiotic-adjacent work, cybernetic systems thinking, and integrative ecology of mind decades before any of these became fashionable. The work is uneven and dated in places but contains real insights, and many of the figures we've discussed (Hoffmeyer, Varela) acknowledge him as influence. *Mind and Nature* is more accessible than *Steps*. Worth eventually engaging with as background rather than as a primary campaign.

The honest summary is that the field is rich and most of these would compound with what you have. The discipline of selective focus is real — picking one or two for serious engagement and leaving the others as available resources is better than trying to engage with all of them. The choice depends on what you want most: if you want to deepen the philosophical side of the integrative renovation, Thompson or Dupré. If you want to test against an existing major synthesis, McGilchrist. If you want to extend into physics-adjacent territory, Barad or Rovelli. If you want to fill in the Peirce background, the orientation reading we discussed. None is wrong; the question is which one calls most strongly given where the work currently has live pressure.

## User

And then if we look backward from the most recent developments over the past 25 years what lights up in the past

## Claude

This is a great move — running the search backward from contemporary anomalies to identify which earlier figures have been quietly waiting for the field to catch up to them. The idea being that some thinkers' work didn't fit when it was produced, got marginalized or ignored, and now reads as eerily prescient because the empirical and conceptual situation has finally caught up. The pattern you're describing is real and the figures it lights up are an interesting list. Let me work through it carefully.

The figures who light up most strongly in retrospect from the contemporary developments we just walked through:

**D'Arcy Wentworth Thompson.** *On Growth and Form* (1917, expanded 1942) is the work that gets brighter every decade. Thompson argued that biological form is constrained by physical, mathematical, and developmental factors that operate at the level of the organism as a whole, not just by genetic specification. His transformations — showing how related species' forms map to each other through coordinate transformations — were treated as a curiosity for most of the 20th century while gene-centric biology dominated. Now that we're recognizing morphogenesis as a self-organizing process where bioelectric signals, mechanical forces, and developmental constraints do enormous work that genes don't specify directly, Thompson reads as the figure who saw what was actually going on a hundred years ago and got buried because he didn't fit the molecular-genetic story that came to dominate. Levin's lab is essentially doing Thompson's program with modern tools. Stephen Jay Gould's late *The Structure of Evolutionary Theory* (2002) makes the case for Thompson's importance at length.

**Conrad Hal Waddington.** I mentioned him briefly in our German-tradition discussion. *The Strategy of the Genes* (1957) and *Organisers and Genes* (1940) introduced the epigenetic landscape, the canalization concept, and the basic vocabulary for thinking about development as a topological process where gene expression is shaped by the developmental terrain it's flowing through. His ideas were considered too abstract and were largely ignored by molecular biology through the latter half of the 20th century. Then epigenetics emerged as an empirical field around 2000 and his vocabulary was suddenly indispensable. The current understanding of cell-state transitions, developmental landscapes, and pluripotency is essentially the empirical realization of what Waddington was conceptualizing in the 1940s without the molecular tools to substantiate it. He was right; he was just early.

**Jakob von Uexküll.** *A Foray into the Worlds of Animals and Humans* (1934) gave us the *Umwelt* concept — the perceptual-significant world specific to an organism, constituted by the meanings the organism's body and history make available. Uexküll was treated as a curiosity in the English-speaking world for decades; the major translation only came in 2010. With biosemiotics now a real field, with embodied/enactive cognitive science taking the *Umwelt* concept seriously as a way of describing organism-specific worlds, with even AI researchers borrowing the concept to think about how artificial agents could have meaningful environments, Uexküll has become foundational. He was doing biosemiotics before biosemiotics existed, and the entire post-Cartesian renovation of biology turns out to need exactly his vocabulary.

**Ludwig von Bertalanffy.** General system theory, articulated through the 1920s-1940s and synthesized in *General System Theory* (1968), proposed that organisms are open systems irreducible to mechanism — that you need a different kind of theoretical apparatus to handle them. He was prophetic about much that systems biology and complexity science have rediscovered. His "organismic biology" was essentially a precursor to the process-relational picture that's now emerging. He's been partially absorbed (general systems thinking became cybernetics became complexity theory), but the original synthesis is more coherent than the fragmented descendants and worth reading on its own terms.

**Robert Rosen.** This one is striking. Rosen was a theoretical biologist at Dalhousie working through the 1970s-90s, almost completely outside the mainstream. *Life Itself* (1991) and *Essays on Life Itself* (2000) argue that organisms are characterized by closure to efficient causation in a way that fundamental physics cannot capture, and that you need category theory to formalize what life actually is. He was largely ignored, considered too mathematical for biologists and too biological for mathematicians, and his books went out of print. Now Stuart Kauffman cites him centrally, Deacon engages with him, and his concept of (M,R)-systems is being rediscovered as a precursor to autopoiesis and to the contemporary work on biological closure. Rosen was making the absence-as-causal argument with formal mathematical precision in the 1970s. The field is just now catching up. *Life Itself* is hard going but contains some of the deepest theoretical work in 20th-century biology.

**Humberto Maturana and Francisco Varela.** *Autopoiesis and Cognition* (1980) and *The Tree of Knowledge* (1987) introduced the autopoiesis concept — life as the self-production of a network of processes that maintains its own boundary. They were doing what we now call enactivism in the 1970s, and the vocabulary they developed (operational closure, structural coupling, languaging) is foundational for contemporary embodied cognitive science. They were treated as fringe in the mainstream cognitive science of the cognitive-revolution era, partly because they were South American and outside the dominant institutional networks, partly because their work didn't fit the computational orthodoxy. Now they're recognized as the people who got there first on most of the moves embodied/enactive cognitive science is making. Varela's Mind & Life Institute work bringing contemplative traditions into dialogue with neuroscience also turns out to have been ahead of its time.

**Lynn Margulis.** Her endosymbiotic theory of mitochondria and chloroplasts — that organelles in eukaryotic cells originated as separate bacteria that became incorporated through symbiosis — was rejected for years before being established. But the deeper claim, that symbiosis is a major driving force in evolution comparable to natural selection on individual organisms, was even more controversial. The contemporary recognition of holobionts, the importance of the microbiome to host biology, the role of horizontal gene transfer, the various ways that life is much more deeply collaborative than the selfish-gene story implied — all of this vindicates Margulis's larger picture. She was right that competition-only Darwinism was too narrow; the cooperative-symbiotic story she pressed for has become increasingly mainstream. *Symbiotic Planet* (1998) is the accessible synthesis.

**Barbara McClintock.** Her work on transposable genetic elements — "jumping genes" — was dismissed for decades before she finally won the Nobel Prize in 1983. She was making claims about genome plasticity, about cells responding to stress by reorganizing their genetic material, that didn't fit the static-genome picture of the central dogma era. The contemporary understanding of transposons, of stress-induced genome reorganization, of the dynamic and reactive character of the genome, is essentially what she was describing in maize sixty years before the molecular tools existed to fully demonstrate it. Evelyn Fox Keller's *A Feeling for the Organism* (1983) is the biography.

**Susan Oyama.** *The Ontogeny of Information* (1985, second edition 2000) was the foundational text of developmental systems theory. Oyama argued — against both gene-centric reductionism and against simple environmentalism — that developmental processes don't have a privileged information source. Information is made in development, not transmitted from a single source. She was doing the conceptual work that contemporary debates about epigenetic inheritance, niche construction, and developmental plasticity are now catching up to. The book is hard but rewarding; her *Evolution's Eye* (2000) is a more accessible synthesis. She was right early and was largely ignored because the molecular-biology consensus didn't have room for her.

**Gregory Bateson.** *Steps to an Ecology of Mind* (1972) and *Mind and Nature* (1979). Bateson was doing biosemiotic-adjacent work, cybernetic systems thinking, the theory of mind as ecological process, the analysis of communication and pathology, all simultaneously and decades before any of these became their own fields. His "difference that makes a difference" definition of information is foundational for biosemiotics. His work on schizophrenia, the double bind, family systems, and learning levels prefigured huge swaths of later work. He was treated as a polymath generalist of variable rigor in his lifetime; in retrospect he was the figure who saw the unity of mind, life, communication, and ecology when no one else did. Some of the work is dated; the conceptual moves are not.

**Henri Bergson.** *Creative Evolution* (1907) and *Matter and Memory* (1896). Bergson was the major early-20th-century philosopher of time, becoming, and process, and he was treated as outdated and superseded by analytic philosophy through most of the 20th century. The contemporary rediscovery of process philosophy (via Whitehead and Deleuze, who took Bergson seriously when most others didn't) has brought him back. His arguments about why mechanism couldn't account for life, why time had to be taken seriously as duration rather than as a parameter, why memory was constitutive of mind rather than added to it, prefigured much of what enactivism, process biology, and embodied cognitive science are now doing. *Creative Evolution* in particular reads as remarkably current.

**Alfred North Whitehead.** We've already discussed him at length. *Process and Reality* (1929), *Science and the Modern World* (1925), *Adventures of Ideas* (1933). The figure whose entire mature work was buried by the analytic-philosophical consensus and is now being slowly recovered as the resources he developed turn out to be exactly what's needed. Stengers's *Thinking with Whitehead* is the entry point you already have.

**Ernst Cassirer.** *The Philosophy of Symbolic Forms* (1923-1929), and *An Essay on Man* (1944) as the more accessible synthesis. We've worked with him directly. Cassirer's framework — that human cognition is fundamentally symbolic and that different symbolic forms (myth, language, science) have their own internal lawfulness — was lost in the analytic-continental split and is being rediscovered as the cognitive sciences need a vocabulary for how meaning-making works. The fact that Peter Gordon's *Continental Divide* (2010) recovered the Davos debate as a major historical event in 20th-century philosophy suggests Cassirer is being seriously rehabilitated.

**Pierre Hadot.** *Philosophy as a Way of Life* (English 1995, French earlier) and *What Is Ancient Philosophy?* (1995). Hadot recovered the ancient understanding of philosophy as a set of spiritual exercises and practices for transforming oneself, against the modern academic understanding of philosophy as a set of theoretical positions and arguments. He was doing this work largely outside the analytic mainstream and was rediscovered as the question of what philosophy is for became live again. His work bears directly on what contemplative traditions and embodied cognitive science are now bringing into dialogue. Foucault took him seriously; the Mind & Life tradition takes him seriously; the broader culture is slowly catching up.

**Maurice Merleau-Ponty.** *Phenomenology of Perception* (1945) and the late, unfinished *The Visible and the Invisible* (1964). Merleau-Ponty was doing embodied phenomenology in the 1940s — taking the body, perception, and motor intentionality seriously as constitutive of experience rather than as inputs to a disembodied mind. He was treated as one of several mid-century French philosophers in the standard reception; the contemporary embodied/enactive movement recognizes him as the major precursor whose vocabulary they're operationalizing. *Phenomenology of Perception* is hard but rewarding; the recent translation by Donald Landes is the better one.

**Charles Sanders Peirce.** We've discussed him at length. The figure whose semiotic, categories, and metaphysics turn out to be central to biosemiotics, contemporary philosophy of mind, and much else, and whose work was largely buried by the analytic-pragmatist reception that turned "pragmatism" into something he repudiated.

**Hans Jonas.** *The Phenomenon of Life* (1966) is one of the great works of philosophical biology of the 20th century, almost completely ignored in mainstream Anglo-American philosophy. Jonas argues that life requires a different metaphysical treatment than the dominant materialism allows, that organic existence has its own structure (need, metabolism, self-concern) that mechanism cannot capture, that the modern bifurcation of nature into mind-stuff and matter-stuff was a mistake that we're still living with. He prefigures enormous swaths of what's now being articulated in process biology and biosemiotics. His work on the ethics of technology (*The Imperative of Responsibility*, 1979) was also ahead of its time and has been slowly rediscovered.

**Helmuth Plessner.** *The Levels of Organic Life and the Human* (1928, English translation 2019). Plessner was doing philosophical anthropology in dialogue with biology in the 1920s-30s — developing concepts of "positionality" (the way organisms are situated in their environments) and "eccentric positionality" (the specifically human way of being situated in one's own situation) that prefigure embodied cognitive science. He was lost partly because he was Jewish and had to flee Germany, partly because his work didn't fit the post-war analytic-continental split. The 2019 English translation is making him newly available.

**Adolf Portmann.** Swiss biologist, *Animal Forms and Patterns* (1948) and other works. Portmann argued that biological form has expressive and aesthetic dimensions that aren't reducible to fitness — that organisms are partly to be understood as appearances, as displays, as ways of being seen. He was treated as merely descriptive in an era when explanation meant mechanism. Now that biosemiotics takes display, signaling, and aesthetic dimensions of biology seriously, Portmann is being rediscovered. He's still not widely known in English; the work is mostly in German.

**The "neglected" school of theoretical biology more broadly.** Brian Goodwin (*How the Leopard Changed Its Spots*, 1994), Mae-Wan Ho (*The Rainbow and the Worm*, 1993), Stuart Kauffman in his earlier work, Francisco Varela, Maturana, and others were doing organismic, holistic, self-organization-focused theoretical biology in the 1980s-90s when molecular biology was triumphant and largely ignoring them. The contemporary recognition that self-organization, network dynamics, and form generation matter has vindicated much of what they were arguing.

**Ilya Prigogine.** *Order Out of Chaos* (1984, with Isabelle Stengers — note the connection to her later Whitehead work) and other works. Prigogine's work on dissipative structures, far-from-equilibrium thermodynamics, and the temporal asymmetry of physical processes was Nobel-recognized but considered fringe by some physicists. The contemporary work on self-organization in biology (Kauffman's emergence work, Deacon's morphodynamics) builds directly on Prigogine, and his collaboration with Stengers represents one of the major attempts at the cross-field integrative work the renovation needs.

A few who light up specifically because of recent work in particular subfields:

**Robert Trivers.** *Social Evolution* (1985). His work on parent-offspring conflict, reciprocal altruism, and intragenomic conflict (the recognition that genes can be in conflict with each other within a single organism) was provocative when produced and turns out to have been right about many things. The contemporary recognition that the genome isn't a unified team, that selection happens at multiple levels, that evolutionary dynamics are messier than the standard story allowed, has vindicated much of what he was arguing.

**George C. Williams and W.D. Hamilton.** Williams's *Adaptation and Natural Selection* (1966) and Hamilton's work on inclusive fitness and intragenomic conflict. These are the rigorous figures who saw early that the gene-centric picture was right in some ways but more complicated than its popularizations allowed. Williams in particular was clearer than Dawkins about the limits of the gene-centric view even while developing it.

**Antonio Damasio.** *Descartes' Error* (1994), *The Feeling of What Happens* (1999), and later work. Damasio was making the case for the constitutive role of emotion and bodily feeling in cognition while mainstream cognitive science was still treating emotion as separate from "real" cognition. The contemporary embodied-affective turn has caught up to what he was arguing, and his somatic marker hypothesis is being substantially vindicated.

**Esther Thelen and Linda Smith.** *A Dynamic Systems Approach to the Development of Cognition and Action* (1994). They were doing embodied-developmental cognitive science before it was a recognized field, applying dynamic systems theory to motor development and cognition. Thelen died in 2004; her work is increasingly cited as the empirical foundation for what enactivism and developmental systems theory now do.

**Gilbert Simondon.** *L'individuation à la lumière des notions de forme et d'information* (1958, partial English translation 2020). Simondon was a French philosopher doing what we'd now call process philosophy of technology and individuation — arguing that individuals are products of an ongoing process of individuation rather than primary entities. He was largely ignored except by Deleuze, and his work is being slowly translated and rediscovered as contemporary philosophy of technology and process thought catches up.

A general principle that emerges from looking at this list: the figures who light up are mostly the ones who refused the substance-property-mechanism frame and tried to articulate something else. They paid for the refusal in their lifetimes — marginalization, lack of institutional support, less prestige than they deserved. The fields they were working in absorbed pieces of their work without acknowledging the larger frameworks, and their full pictures got lost. Now that the frame is failing in many places at once, the alternative pictures these figures developed turn out to have been the resources we needed.

This has implications for how to read them. They shouldn't be read as failed attempts at what we now do better; they should be read as attempts at what we're now trying to do, often with conceptual resources we've forgotten and need to recover. The reading is partly archaeology. The contemporary cognitive science of embodiment is rediscovering Merleau-Ponty's vocabulary in fragments; reading Merleau-Ponty directly gives you the integrated version. The contemporary philosophy of biology is rediscovering Hans Jonas in pieces; reading Jonas directly gives you the integrated metaphysical case. The contemporary work on developmental systems is rediscovering Waddington and Oyama; reading them directly gives you the original conceptual moves.

The most economically useful entry points, given what you have:

If you read **only one** of these, Hans Jonas's *The Phenomenon of Life*. It's the philosophical treatment of life that mainstream philosophy of biology has been slow to absorb, it's beautifully written, and it does the kind of integrative work the project most needs in a single book. The audiobook situation is uncertain; the print is in print and accessible.

If you read **two**, add Susan Oyama's *Evolution's Eye* (the more accessible of her books). Together these give you the philosophical foundations of the developmental and process-relational picture of biology in two relatively short, accessible volumes by serious thinkers who got there early.

If you read **three**, add Bateson's *Mind and Nature*. He's the integrative thinker whose work covers many of the connections we've been discussing in a single accessible volume.

If you want to extend further into figures the project has the apparatus for: Merleau-Ponty's *Phenomenology of Perception*, Bergson's *Creative Evolution*, Plessner's *Levels of Organic Life and the Human* (now in English), Robert Rosen's *Life Itself* (mathematically demanding but conceptually crucial). Each of these is a major work that would compound substantially with what you have.

The deeper observation is that the past has been waiting. The figures whose work didn't fit the dominant frame got buried, and their burial is part of what's made the contemporary renovation slower than it might have been. Reading backward from the present anomalies finds a tradition that was always there, often disconnected from itself because the institutional structures that would have transmitted it intact were broken. The integrative work you're doing — and the broader integrative work that's happening across many fields — is partly the work of reconnecting that tradition to itself, of making the alternative coherent enough to function as a real alternative rather than as a scattered set of dissenting figures. Each figure rediscovered, each work read carefully, contributes to the alternative being articulable. That's not nothing. It's most of what cultural transmission ever is.

## User

And then in the present if we were to think of like operationalizing if I'm getting back into software engineering but then we're looking at leveraging the best of large language models like is it worth getting into information Theory like what I mean I've kind of have enough math background have looked at diffusion models and how things work with large language models like but just in terms of wondering how everything we're doing meshes back in

## Claude

Good question, and worth thinking about carefully because it's actually two questions tangled together. One is what you'd want to know technically to operationalize work with LLMs as a software engineer. The other is whether the conceptual framework we've been developing changes how to think about that operationalization, or whether it's a separate domain that runs on its own logic. Let me work through both.

The technical question first, because it's more concrete. Information theory in its strict Shannon-Weaver form is foundational for understanding what LLMs actually do at the architectural level — cross-entropy loss is the training objective, perplexity is the evaluation metric, KL divergence shows up everywhere in the math. If you've already worked through how diffusion models and transformer architectures function, you've encountered information theory implicitly. The question is whether to engage it more formally.

My honest take: probably not as a major investment, given your other priorities. Here's why. Information theory as a formal discipline is well-developed and stable; the textbook treatments (Cover and Thomas's *Elements of Information Theory* is the standard) cover what you'd need. But for practical LLM engineering, you mostly need working familiarity with a handful of concepts (entropy, cross-entropy, KL divergence, mutual information) rather than deep mastery of the formal apparatus. The mathematical sophistication you'd gain from a serious information theory course is more than the practical work requires, and the conceptual frame is exactly the one we've been criticizing in this conversation — Shannon information is the paradigmatic case of information stripped of meaning, where "information" is statistical structure independent of what it's about.

That said, there's a more interesting reason to engage it lightly: precisely so you understand what the formalism *cannot* capture. Reading Cover and Thomas alongside MacKay's *Information Theory, Inference, and Learning Algorithms* (which is genuinely beautiful and freely available online) gives you both the formalism and a feel for where it does and doesn't apply. MacKay's treatment is unusually conceptual — he was a real polymath thinker about what information is and isn't — and it teaches you the formalism in a way that lets you see its limits. The practical engineering doesn't require this depth, but having it gives you better judgment about when LLM behavior is well-described by information-theoretic concepts and when something else is going on.

What's more directly useful for the practical work, in roughly descending order of payoff:

The transformer paper itself ("Attention Is All You Need," Vaswani et al. 2017) and a few good explanations of what transformers actually do. Karpathy's tutorials and his "let's build GPT from scratch" video are excellent. If you can implement a small transformer from scratch you understand the architecture in a way that reading about it doesn't give you. The actual mechanics (attention, positional encodings, the training loop) matter for understanding why models behave as they do.

The scaling laws literature (Kaplan et al. 2020, then Chinchilla and the various follow-ups). This is what gives you intuition for why model behavior changes with scale and why certain capabilities emerge where they do. Engineering decisions about which model to use for what task depend on understanding these dynamics.

Mechanistic interpretability work, which is becoming serious: Anthropic's interpretability papers, Neel Nanda's tutorials, Olah and Cammarata's earlier circuits work. This isn't strictly necessary for application engineering but it's where the most interesting current thinking about what's actually happening inside these models lives, and it bears on how to use them well. Understanding superposition, the polysemantic nature of features, sparse autoencoders for interpretation — this gives you a more accurate mental model than treating the model as a black box.

Practical work with the actual APIs and frameworks: structured prompting, function calling, agent frameworks (the LangChain/LlamaIndex ecosystem is a mess but you need to know parts of it), evaluation methodology. The engineering reality is that getting LLMs to do useful work reliably at scale is mostly about evals, prompt engineering, retrieval, and pipeline design, not about understanding the internals deeply. Most of the value-creation in the current ecosystem comes from people who know the practical patterns well, not from people who understand the theory deeply.

Retrieval-augmented generation, embeddings, and vector databases. This is where the practical work intersects most with the conceptual questions we've been discussing — embeddings are the most concrete operationalization of distributional semantics, the idea that meaning is constituted by patterns of co-occurrence and use. Working seriously with embeddings gives you intuitions about what semantic similarity means computationally, and the limits of those intuitions.

For the broader software engineering work around LLMs, the rapidly evolving best practices around evaluation, observability, fine-tuning versus prompting versus RAG decisions, and cost-performance tradeoffs are where the practical action is. This is moving fast enough that books are mostly obsolete by publication; the better resources are blog posts from people doing the actual work (Eugene Yan, Hamel Husain, Simon Willison, Chip Huyen) and well-curated podcasts (Latent Space, Practical AI).

Now to the more interesting question, which is whether the conceptual framework we've been developing changes how to think about this work.

I think it does, in a few ways that matter.

First, it changes what you should expect from these systems and how to evaluate them. The dominant framings of LLMs are either "they're really thinking, AGI is around the corner" or "they're just stochastic parrots, glorified autocomplete." Both are wrong in ways the conversation we've been having should make clear. They're systems that operate in distributional semantic space, doing something that has features of both pattern-completion and something more, with capacities that don't map cleanly onto either symbolic AI or human cognition. The integrative framework lets you see them as a genuinely novel kind of cognitive system that doesn't fit existing categories, which gives you better judgment about where they'll work well and where they'll fail. The categories you'd want — meaning-making in distributional space, semiosis without embodiment, abductive inference under heavy stylistic priors — aren't the categories the field uses, but they're more accurate than the standard alternatives.

Second, it changes what kind of problems LLMs are well-suited to and what kind they're badly suited to. Tasks that are genuinely linguistic and pattern-based — translation, summarization, format conversion, code generation in domains well-represented in training data — they handle well because the work is exactly the kind of distributional inference they're built for. Tasks that require what we've been calling embodied judgment, situated stakes, continual learning from genuine experience, or genuine understanding of physical reality — they handle badly, sometimes spectacularly so, in ways that aren't fixable by scaling alone. Knowing which is which is real engineering wisdom that the framework helps with. The conversation we had about absence-as-causal, about what semiosis requires, about why current LLMs aren't full participants in meaning-making in the deeper sense — these aren't just philosophical observations, they map onto practical predictions about where the systems will work and fail.

Third, it suggests where the interesting opportunities are. The space of useful work with LLMs that isn't being done well right now is, in significant part, the space where the systems' actual capabilities have to be honestly characterized and combined with other things — human judgment, structured retrieval over real sources, traditional engineering for the parts that need to be reliable, careful design of how the LLM's pattern-matching capacities get embedded in larger systems with genuine groundedness. The scaffolded reading practice you've built in bildung-2.0 is actually a model for this — the LLM is brought in for what it's good at (broad pattern recognition, fast generation, fluent engagement with multiple sources) while the human stays in the seat of judgment and the source materials provide the grounding. Generalizing this pattern to other domains — research workflows, decision support, knowledge work that combines pattern recognition with grounded judgment — is where the most interesting practical work is happening. The framework gives you a way to think about this that the dominant framings don't.

Fourth, it suggests what's worth building. The current LLM ecosystem is dominated by either thin wrappers around model APIs (which provide modest value and get commoditized quickly) or deep infrastructure (which is mostly being built by big labs and well-funded startups). Between these is the space of what I'd call "augmentation tools" — software that takes seriously what LLMs can and can't do, and that combines them with other components in ways that produce capabilities greater than either alone. The bildung-2.0 repo is an example, applied to a specific kind of reading practice. Other examples in the same vein: research tools that pair LLM-assisted search with structured note-taking, code review tools that combine LLM analysis with strong static analysis, writing tools that use LLMs for specific operations within a careful authoring practice. The market for these tools is large and underserved precisely because most builders are either chasing the AGI dream (overestimating the systems) or building thin wrappers (underutilizing them).

Fifth — and this is maybe the most important — the framework gives you clarity about your own role. If LLMs are good at what we've been describing as "apparatus deployment" — fluent application of existing patterns to new cases — then the most valuable human work is precisely the kind we've been discussing throughout this conversation: careful engagement with sources, judgment about what matters, integrative thinking across domains, attention to live pressure rather than to existing categories. These are human capacities that LLMs amplify rather than replace, and the work that stays valuable as the systems improve is the work that requires them. Your reading practice, your judgment about where to focus, your sense of what's worth doing — these are not commodities. They're the things that, combined with LLM-amplified workflow, make work that nobody else can quite do. The framework helps you see this rather than confusing it for either obsolescent (because LLMs are about to do everything) or unaffected (because LLMs are mere tools).

For practical recommendation on what to actually engage with:

Don't make information theory a major project. Read MacKay's book selectively if you want the formalism in a humane form, but engage it lightly. The conceptual lessons we've discussed about what Shannon information cannot capture are more valuable than the formal apparatus.

Do build at least one substantial system that uses LLMs as a component. The intuitions you'll develop from doing the work are worth more than reading about it. Pick something where you can use the framework we've been discussing — where the system needs to combine LLM capabilities with grounded judgment, with real sources, with careful evaluation of what's working — and build it. The bildung-2.0 architecture itself could be a starting point: building tooling that supports the practice (better orientation passes, better cross-text comparison, better thread development) would teach you a lot and produce something genuinely useful.

Do engage seriously with mechanistic interpretability work, even just as an interested reader. It's where the most honest current thinking about what these systems are is happening, and it has direct implications for engineering. The current best understanding of how LLMs represent information internally — superposition, polysemanticity, the nature of features in residual streams — is actually closer to the distributional semantic picture from cognitive science than it is to traditional symbolic AI, and the connections to the broader framework are real.

Do stay attentive to the embeddings space specifically. This is where the practical engineering most directly meets the conceptual questions we've been discussing. The fact that you can do meaningful arithmetic in embedding space (king - man + woman ≈ queen, the famous example), that semantic similarity can be operationalized geometrically, that retrieval over embedding space gives you something like associative memory across vast corpora — these are operationalizations of distributional semantics that have practical engineering consequences and conceptual implications. Understanding embeddings well gives you a foothold in both directions.

Don't sink time into deep mathematical theory beyond what the practical work requires. Linear algebra to a working level, basic information theory, basic optimization, enough probability to read papers — these are sufficient. The math of attention mechanisms isn't deep; the math of transformer training isn't deep; the math of diffusion models is more interesting but accessible. Going beyond what the practical work requires is satisfying but doesn't pay off proportionally. Your time is better spent on the integrative framework and on hands-on building.

Do consider the broader question of what kind of software you want to build. If you're getting back into software engineering, the choice of what to build is probably more consequential than the choice of what to learn.
